==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Motivation: Annotating breathing events in polysomnographic (PSG) recordings is a time-consuming
and costly process with a high inter-scorer variability. The primary aim (1) of the current study
was to develop an automatic algorithm for detection of Cheyne-Stokes breathing (CSB), charac-
terized by repetitive waxing and waning breathing patterns, to overcome the problems of manual
scoring. As an extension to the project, a secondary aim (2) was added to investigate whether the
CSB predictions could be used to classify heart failure (HF) vs. no-HF patients as a way of testing
the correlation between CSB and HF shown in the literature.
Data: 1) A total of 442 PSG recordings were selected from four diﬀerent sleep study cohorts of
which the thermistor, nasal canulla, thoracic belt, abdominal belt, oximetry, and chin electromyo-
graphy (EMG) signals were included. The signals were annotated for CSB by three external sleep
technicians. 2) The same modalities were extracted from a second data set consisting of 443 PSG
recordings from the MrOS Sleep Study of which 143 were HF patients.
Method: 1) The developed CSB detection algorithm was initiated with a pre-processing procedure
composed of ﬁltering, resampling, outlier removal, and normalization to allow comparison between
diﬀerent signals and cohorts. From the pre-processed signals, features were extracted from the tem-
poral domain and split into equal length segments, discarding the segments with a low signal/noise
ratio. The segments were used as input to a transformer inspired deep neural network designed
to make sample-wise predictions, and the network output was post-processed to get the ﬁnal CSB
classiﬁcation in accordance with the annotation rules used by the sleep technicians. 2) A set of
features were extracted from the predicted CSB segments designed to reﬂect CSB severity. These
features were passed into a support vector machine (SVM) with a radial basis function (RBF)
kernel in a 10-fold cross-validation scheme with the purpose of classifying HF. Secondly, the most
discriminative feature was selected, and the optimal feature threshold for distinguishing between
HF patients and no-HF patients was found based on a receiver operator curve (ROC) using half
the data set. The other half of the data set was split at the threshold value and the odds ratio
(OR) and conﬁdence interval (CI) was computed between the two constructed groups.
Results: 1) The algorithm achieved an average f1-score of 0.76 on the test set when comparing
with a consensus score, which was almost at the same level as the lowest performing sleep technician
(0.78). There were also a high agreement between the descriptive statistics of the CSB predictions
and the consensus annotations. The algorithm performed worse when predicting subjects with a
high obstructive apnea hypopnea index (OAHI) than a low OAHI. 2) The HF classiﬁer reached an
accuracy of 69.4 % only slightly above what could be expected from guessing by chance (65.9 %)
even though the OR was found to be signiﬁcant at 1.97 (CI of 1.10 and 3.5).
Conclusion: 1) The presented CSB algorithm proved to have a high accuracy, and with minor
alterations and a conﬁrmation of the results on a secondary data set it has the potential to be used
as a screening tool in a clinical setting or as a scientiﬁc tool for researching CSB in large data bases.
2) While the OR shows that there is potential for classifying HF patients, the feature and classiﬁer
design should be improved to achieve better class separation and even then the task might prove
too diﬃcult to solve using only breathing patterns.

==PAGE:4==
Resumé
Motivation: Annotering af vejrtrækningsforstyrrelser i polysomnograﬁske optagelser (PSG) er en
dyr og tidskrævende opgave med en høj variabilitet i udførelsen mellem søvnteknikere. Det primære
mål (1) med dette studie var at udvikle en automatisk algoritme til detektion af Cheyne-Stokes
breathing (CSB), karakteriseret ved repetitivt stigende og faldende vejrtrækningsmønster, for at
takle problemerne med manuel scoring. Som en forlængelse af projektet blev et sekundært mål (2)
tilføjet for at teste om CSB prædiktionerne kunne blive brugt til at klassiﬁcere hjertesvigt (HF) vs.
no-HF patienter som en test af korrelationen mellem CSB og HF, vist i tidligere studier.
Data: 1) 442 PSG optagelser blev selekteret fra 4 forskellige søvnkohorter, hvorfra termistor-,
næsekanyle-, thoraxbælte-, abdomenbælte-, oximetri-, og hage electromyograﬁsignalet blev inklud-
eret. Signalerne blev annoteret for CSB af tre eksterne søvnteknikere. 2) De samme modaliteter
blev ekstraheret fra et sekundært datasæt bestående af 443 PSG optagelser fra MrOS Sleep Study,
hvoraf 143 var HF patienter.
Metode: 1) Den udviklede algoritme blev innitieret med en pre-processerings procedure bestående
af ﬁltrering, resampling, fjernelse af anormaliteter og normalisering for at kunne sammenligne
forskellige signaler og kohorter. Fra de præ-processerede signaler ekstraheredes features fra det
temporale domæne og de blev segmenteret, hvor segmenter med et dårligt signal/støj forhold blev
fjernet. Segmenterne blev brugt som input til et transformerinspireret dybt neuralt netværk de-
signet der prædikterede sample-vis CSB. Netværk outputtet blev post-processeret for at opnå,
at den endelige CSB-klassiﬁcering var i overensstemmelse med annotationsreglerne brugt af søvn-
teknikerne 2) Et sæt features blev extraheret fra de prædikerede CSB segmenter, som var designet
til at reﬂektere alvorligheden af CSB. Disse features blev processeret i en support vector machine
med en radial basis funktion (RBF) kernel i en 10-fold krydsvalidering med formålet at klassiﬁcere
HF. Derudover blev den mest diskriminative feature valgt, og den optimale grænseværdi til at
adskille HF- fra ikke HF-patienter blev fundet baseret på en modtageroperatorkurve (ROC) lavet
med halvdelen af datasættet. Den anden halvdel af datasættet blev splittet ved grænseværdien,
hvorpå odds ratioen (OR) og konﬁdensintervallet (CI) blev udregnet mellem de to grupper.
Resultater: 1) Algoritmen opnåede en gennemsnitlig f1-score på 0.76 på testsættet holdt op
mod en konsensusscore, hvilket var næsten på højde med den lavest performende søvntekniker
(0.78). Der var også en høj lighed mellem de deskriptive statistikker for CSB prædiktionerne og
konsensusannotationerne. Algoritmen præsterede dårligere for subjekter med et obstruktiv apnø
hypapnø indeks (OAHI) end en lav OAHI. 2) HF klassiﬁkatoren opnåede en nøjagtighed på 69,4
%, hvilket kun var en smule bedre end hvad der kunne forventes af at gætte tilfældigt (65.9 %),
selvom OR viste sig at være signiﬁkant på 1.97 (CI på 1.10 og 3.5)
Konklusion: 1) Den udviklede CSB algoritme havde en høj nøjagtighed og med mindre æn-
dringer samt en bekræftelse af resultaterne på et sekundært datasæt, har den potentialet til at
blive brugt som klinisk screenings redskab eller som videnskabeligt redskab til at undersøge CSB i
store datasæt. 2) OR værdien viste, at der er måske er mulighed for at klassiﬁcere HF patienter,
men både feature- og klassiﬁkatordesignet skal forbedres for at opnå en bedre separation mellem
klasserne, og selv da er opgaven muligvis for svær til at løse.
iii
==PAGE:5==
Preface
This master thesis project is a result of a joint trans-Atlantic collaboration between Department
of Health Technology at the Technical University of Denmark, Division of sleep at Department of
Psychiatry and Behavioral Sciences at Stanford University and Rigshospitalet Glostrup. The thesis
constitutes part of the requirements for obtaining a master degree within the ﬁeld of Biomedical
Engineering from The Technical University of Denmark.
Together with an oral defense of the
project, it makes up a total of 30 ECTS points.
The project presents and discusses the development of an algorithm for automatic detection of
Cheyne-Stokes breathing and heart failure and the background theory necessary to understand it.
To keep the thesis as concise as possible, the explanations and equations have been written to be
understood by other master students and researchers within the same ﬁeld . The developed code
has been well documented and it has been strived to make the code work “straight-out-of-the-box”
if other researchers should desire to reproduce the results or expand the algorithm.

Acknowledgements
Many people have contributed to this scientiﬁc work and I would like to take this page out to
properly thank everybody that have been involved.
First, I would like to thank my main supervisor associate professor, Helge Bjarrup Dissing
Sørensen, for giving me an opportunity to conduct research at one of the most prestigious Univer-
sities in the world where I have been able to collaborate with ﬁeld leading scientist. This chance
was given despite of a background outside of engineering and a big thank you should be given for
trusting my knowledge and ability to put in the necessary work.
I would also like to thank professor, Emmanuel Mignot, for his patient counseling and advice
including the endless brainstorming sessions that has helped keep my motivation high throughout
the project.
A thank you should also be given to professor, Poul Jennum, for his help in improving my clinical
understanding of sleep disordered breathing and the complex underlying pathophysiology.
I also have to acknowledge that this project couldn’t have been completed without the data
made available by the National Sleep Research Resource. They have not only supplied data for the
project but they have also been so kind to answer any question I have had regarding the deﬁnitions
etc. surrounding the data set. In extension to this praise, I should thank Ph.D Eileen B. Leary,
who helped me get data permissions and was very welcoming when I initially arrived in the states
and to the external sleep technicians Margie Barracas, Sheila Mae Villaruz-Morte and Kelvin Roy
Terminez lead by Glenn Roldal who annotated the data for Cheyne-Stokes Breathing.
Lastly, a big thank you to my roommate and fellow master student Villads Hulgaard Jørgensen
for countless technical discussions and to my roommate and technical supervisor Ph.D. student
Umaer Rashid Hanif for sharing his experience and knowledge along the way, the project would
not have had the same level without you guys, and neither would the stay abroad.

Chapter 1
Introduction
The inﬂuence of sleep quality on overall health has become an increasingly discussed topic in
the public over the course of the last couple of decades. This has been accelerated by repeating
revelations from the scientiﬁc community showing strong correlations between sleep quality and
important health parameters such as hypertension, excessive day-time sleeping, stroke, coronary
heart disease, heart failure and even increased mortality [6, 7]. A common reason for reduced sleep
quality is sleep disordered breathing (SDB), covering several pathophysiologies of which the most
common have been estimated to have a prevalence of around 12 % in the American population [8, 9].
Diagnosing these patients is a time-consuming and costly process because it requires an overnight
sleep recording and as a result of that the majority of the patients never get diagnosed [10]. The
costs burden of undiagnosed cases on the American society has been estimated to be 149.6 billion
dollars in 2015 and this number is thought to increase as obesity becomes an increasing problem
[9]. To lower this ﬁgure signiﬁcantly and ramp up testing would require a reformation of the cur-
rent diagnostic approach, and this could be lead by deep learning. Deep learning is becoming an
increasingly important method within biomedical engineering as it is being used heavily in modern
image based screening tools such as breast tumor detection in mammography or separating benign
from malicious skin cancer [11, 12]. Data driven methods like deep learning has the advantage of
avoiding human error and bias as well as reducing the time needed to process enormous amounts
of data, two problems that are common when diagnosing sleep disordered breathing [13]. At the
moment automated data driven processes are not trusted to a degree where they can be used as
diagnostic tools but as the scientiﬁc community strives to improve the performance and trans-
parency of these algorithms, they will presumably also become an essential part of diagnosing sleep
disordered breathing.
1.0.1
Project Statement
CSB is a type of sleep disordered breathing commonly observed in heart failure patients, persons
exposed to high altitude and patients with brain lesions in the respiratory center and it manifests
as a unique crescendo-decrescendo pattern interspersed with central sleep apneas or central sleep
hypopneas. The golden standard for recognizing and diagnosing CSB is costly and time-consuming
and previous studies have tried to develop algorithms for predicting these breathing pattern but
with diﬀerent and often limited levels of success [1, 2, 4, 5, 14–16]. The initial and main purpose
1
==PAGE:17==
2
of this thesis was to answer the question: How can deep learning methods be used to automatically
detect segments of CSB in overnight recordings?, while concurrently developing a novel algorithm
for detection of CSB. As an extension introduced late in the project it was decided to investigate
the correlation between heart failure and segments of periodic breathing during sleep. Uncovering
both of these areas of scientiﬁc interest could have the potential to not only introduce new screening
tools to the sleep clinic but also shed more light on the link between heart failure and breathing
during sleep.
1.0.2
Project Aims
The aims of the current project were derived from the introduction and project statement above
as well as the state-of-the-art review (see chapter 4):
• To import and pre-process signals from four diﬀerent sleep research cohorts with the purpose
of making the signals comparable.
• To implement advanced biomedical signal processing techniques to extract discriminative
features from the signals, which contain information on the presence and position of CSB.
• To develop and design a deep learning network suited for predicting the exact position of
CSB, and to attempt an interpretation of the internal network decision rules.
• To perform adequate post-processing of the network output and compare the results with the
golden standard as well as state-of-the-art literature.
• To design the ﬁrst edition of a machine learning based algorithm that could separate HF
subjects from no-HF subjects and act as a basis for discussion and further research.
==PAGE:18==
Chapter 2
Clinical Background
This chapter gives a short overview of the clinical background of the project. Focus is emphasized on
describing the medical background knowledge that is necessary to comprehend the coupling between
technical features and physiological/pathophysiological parameters and the reasoning behind the
methodical decisions. The chapter will start by reviewing breathing mechanisms and regulations
during sleep, followed by a section on data recording during sleep and end with a description of
SDB.
2.1
Breathing during sleep
Figure 2.1: Illustration of breathing mechanics. When
the intercostal muscles and the diaphragm contracts air
is inhaled and when they relax air is exhaled. Taken from
www.thoughtco.com [17]
Breathing is one of many vital functions in the
human body. It is the method by which the or-
ganism exchanges oxygen (O2) and carbon diox-
ide (CO2) between the blood and the air. The
mechanics of breathing works by adjusting the
pressure inside the lungs as can be seen in ﬁg-
ure 2.1. The diaphragm and the chest wall is
attached to the structures of the lung, so that
when the diaphragm and the intercostal mus-
cles contract and expand downwards and out-
wards, the volume increases in the lung leading
to a pressure fall as described by Boyle’s law in
equation 2.1 (describing the behavior of a ﬁxed
amount of gas at a constant temperature):
P ∝1
V
(2.1)
where P is pressure and V is volume. When
the pressure falls inside the lungs, air ﬂows from the higher pressure in the ambient air into the lungs
3
==PAGE:19==
Chapter 2. Clinical Background
4
and gas exchange is made possible. At exhalation, the opposite happens. The respiratory muscles
relax leading to an increase in pressure and air outﬂow (in a forceful and voluntary exhalation
respiratory muscles can contract in this phase as well) [18].
Figure 2.2: Negative feedback loop of the metabolic
breathing control. Taken from www.quora.com [19]
Breathing is controlled from the respiratory
centers in the pons and medulla oblongata
which are regulated by the metabolic contents
of the blood, mechanoreceptors in the chest wall
and suprapontine stimuli. At the onset of sleep
the suprapontine stimuli disappear and for that
reason their inﬂuence on breathing will not be
further discussed here [20].
Neither will the
mechanoreceptors due to their lack of relevance
for sleep-related disorders.
The metabolites in the blood (i.e. O2 and CO2)
regulates breathing during sleep by a negative
feedback loop, sending signals from peripheral
and central chemoreceptors (controller gain) to
the respiratory centers and from there to the
respiratory muscles (eﬀector mechanism). The
eﬀect of the feedback loop is in turn registered
by the chemoreceptors, but with a delay me-
diated by the blood circulation time (see ﬁg-
ure 2.2) [6, 21, 22].
Due to this mechanism,
a drop in arterial partial pressure of oxygen
(PaO2) or an increase in arterial partial pres-
sure of carbon dioxide (PaCO2) will lead to a
rise in minute ventilation, while the opposite
will lead to a drop in minute ventilation.
In
the sleeping state, healthy individuals have a
PaCO2 set point of approximately 45 mmHg
with an apnea threshold about 5 mmHg below.
The apnea threshold marks the point at which
the neural drive to the inspiratory muscles cease
[6, 20]. This buﬀer is called the CO2 reserve and it prevents apneas from happening due to small
ﬂuctuations in the breathing pattern.
2.2
Polysomnography
Polysomnography (PSG) is the gold standard of medical measuring methods commonly used in sleep
science to retrieve an overnight sleep recording of a subject. A PSG consists of many modalities
that can be interpreted in combination or separately to diagnose sleep-related disorders or deter-
mine sleep quality. The American Association of Sleep Medicine (AASM) recommends using the
following modalities but more could be added to clarify a speciﬁc problem: electroencephalogram
(EEG), electrooculogram, chin electromyography (EMG), leg EMG, airﬂow signals, respiratory
eﬀort signals, oxygen saturation, body position and electrocardiogram [23]. In this project the
airﬂow signals (i.e. thermistor signal and nasal pressure), the respiratory eﬀort signal (i.e. induc-
==PAGE:20==
Chapter 2. Clinical Background
5
tance plethysmography signals), Chin EMG, and oximetry signals have been used, and these will
be further described below. After an over-night recording, the signals are analysed in 30 second
segments by sleep technicians to determine sleep stages, irregular breathing events as well as other
events that might be of interest to the diagnosis.
Figure 2.3:
Illustration of an integrated thermistor
and nasal cannula. Taken from https://www.ternimed.de
[24]
Figure 2.4: Illustration of the attachment of the induc-
tance plethysmography. Taken from wikipedia.org [25]
A PSG is a costly and time consuming mea-
surement to conduct since it has to be a whole-
night recording and because analysis of such a
huge amount of data is a very time consum-
ing process for the medical doctors. Therefore,
this procedure is only oﬀered to patients, where
the medical doctors have a strong assumption
of sleep-related disorders.
It is typically con-
ducted at a medical sleep clinic, due to the
equipment requirements, at the cost of intro-
ducing a bias because the patient might show
a diﬀerent sleeping pattern when sleeping in a
new setting.
According to the AASM scoring manual all the
signals relevant to this study should be sampled
at a minimum of 25 Hz. [23].
Thermistor Signal
A thermistor is a temperature resistor that is
positioned over the mouth and nose to detect
changes in temperature (see ﬁgure 2.3).
It
can monitor the exhalations and inhalations be-
cause the temperature of the air coming from
the lungs is warmer than the temperature of
the ambient air. The signal is typically used to
detect complete stop of airﬂow but it has been
shown to be unreliable when predicting more
subtle airﬂow reductions [26–28].
Nasal Pressure Signal
The nasal pressure signal is recorded with a
nasal cannula positioned inside the nostrils (see
ﬁgure 2.3). It was originally introduced to detect the ﬂow reductions that could not be detected
by the thermistor, and it is now used on a regular basis. It measures the pressure changes inside
the nostrils, and it has been shown to reveal more events than both the thermistor and the induc-
tance plethysmography, especially when looking at small ﬂow changes. It should however be used
together with the thermistor as it doesn’t monitor alterations in the oral breathing [26–28].
==PAGE:21==
Chapter 2. Clinical Background
6
Inductance Plethysmography Signals
The inductance plethysmography signals are utilized to give an estimate of breathing eﬀort. The
signals are also referred to as belt signals, because they consist of two elastic belts with interwoven
coiled wires. When a current is applied to the coils, the expansion and reduction in belt circumfer-
ence can be detected by changes in resistance through the wire [28]. One belt is placed around the
thorax and another one is placed around the abdomen as seen in ﬁgure 2.4, in order to estimate
rib cage movement and abdomen movement separately.
Oximetry
Oxygen saturation is typically measured by pulse oximetry at the tip of the index ﬁnger, and it
is an important signal when searching for hypopneas which are coupled with desaturations. This
peripheral assessment of oxygen saturation is not the golden standard but it is used because the
golden standard involves surgically invasive arterial catheters. The pulse oximeter works by emitting
light at diﬀerent wavelength through the ﬁnger, checking how much is absorbed and exploiting the
fact that oxyhemoglobin and de-oxyhemoglobin absorbs red and infrared light diﬀerently. If the
ratio between absorbed red and infrared light is high then the oxygen saturation is low and vice
versa. The measured oxygen saturations are averaged over a window with a length of 3 to 21
seconds depending on the equipment [26–28].
Chin Electromyography
Chin EMG is used by sleep technicians as one of the signals by which to score sleep staging as the
muscle activity gradually decreases from light non-rapid eye movement (REM) sleep, to deep non-
REM, to REM. The EMG sensor measures the electrical current running along the sarcolemma
during muscle activity and for chin EMG it is positioned 2 cm below the inferior line of the
mandibula and 2 cm to the left and the right of the midline [27, 28].
2.3
Sleep-Disordered Breathing
SDB is a term that describes a spectrum of sleep-related disorders, of which some of the most
frequently observed are Obstructive Sleep Apnea (OSA), Central Sleep Apnea (CSA) and Mixed
Sleep Apnea. All types of sleep apnea are characterized by a cessation of airﬂow ≥90 % to and
from the lungs for a period of time, but the manifestations and pathophysiologies are quite diﬀerent
[6, 29]. The types of apnea also exist in milder versions, termed hypopneas, in which the airﬂow
is reduced to less than 30 %. Lastly, there is a special subtype of CSA called CSB. SDB is tightly
correlated with a reduction of sleep quality, leading to day-time drowsiness, memory deﬁciency, as
well as an increased risk of developing several other life-shortening comorbidities [29].
2.3.1
Obstructive- and Central Sleep apnea
Patients with OSA experiences repetitive collapses of the upper airways making the reduction in
airﬂow a mechanical eﬀect of a reduced space. While the collapse of the airways make inspiration
impossible, the large respiratory muscles of the thorax and the abdomen keeps contracting in a
rhythmical pattern in an attempt to breathe but without any eﬀect. An OSA event typically with
an arousal, leading to an increased neural drive to the muscles around the pharynx which open
the upper airways and lead to sudden powerful inspirations. Figure 2.5 shows an example of a
==PAGE:22==
Chapter 2. Clinical Background
7
PSG recording where the patient experiences several OSA events [6]. Factors such as obesity, age,
gender, gene pool, smoking habits and alcohol use have been correlated with OSA. Of these factors,
especially obesity has been shown to predispose for development of OSA [29].
Figure 2.5: PSG measurement illustrating the repetitive pattern of OSA. Reprinted from [6]
In CSA the cessation of airﬂow happens due to a reduced neural drive to the respiratory muscles.
There are several distinct pathophysiologies that can cause slight variations in the manifestations of
this phenomenon, but for the scope of this report, CSA will be split into a hypercapnic group and
an eucapnic/hypocapnic group (ﬁgure 2.6) [22, 29]. Patients with hypercapnic CSA show an erratic
breathing pattern with varying degree of irregular ventilation. These patients typically suﬀer from
another neurological disease associated with the respiratory centers in medulla oblongata or the
neural pathways leading to the respiratory muscles (e.g. brain lesions or neuropathy). Patients
in the eucapnic/hypocapnic group often have a more regular cyclic breathing pattern which has
to do with the underlying pathophysiology. In eucapnic/hypocapnic CSA an instability occurs in
the homeostatic system, typically due to some degree of constant hyperventilation. This lowers
the PaCO2 pressure closer to the apnea threshold, and thus increases the risk of exceeding the
threshold, stopping the neural drive to the respiratory muscles and experiencing a CSA event.
On top of that, patients often show a high loop gain, meaning an elevated sensitivity to PaCO2
increases, further destabilizing the system and at times causing CSB [6, 22].
==PAGE:23==
Chapter 2. Clinical Background
8
Figure 2.6: An illustration of the breathing patterns associated with the two subtypes of CSA. Note that while
both signals show periodicity, only the top signal can be categorized as CSB characterized by the crescendo/de-
crescendo pattern. Reprinted from [22]
2.3.2
Cheyne-Stokes Breathing
CSB is a pattern of breathing where the patient has CSA events and hyperpneas interchangeably
in a cyclical manner, as depicted in signal a in ﬁgure 2.6. The AASM has deﬁned CSB as an event
requiring both of the following points to be true:
• "There are episodes of ≥3 consecutive central apneas and/or central hypopneas separated
by a crescendo and decrescendo change in breathing amplitude with a cycle length of ≥40
seconds."
• "There are ≥5 central apneas and/or central hypopneas per hour of sleep associated with the
crescendo/decrescendo breathing pattern recorded over ≥2 hours of monitoring." [23]
This deﬁnition will be referred to as the gold standard deﬁnition throughout this report. This
deﬁnition of CSB is not to be confused with periodic breathing (PB), which is a term that is
commonly and loosely used in the literature. No clear deﬁnition can be found in any article, but
in general the term PB seems to be used as a description of an event in a signal that has the same
crescendo/decrescendo pattern of periodicity, but where the apneas can be obstructive as well as
central.
As described above, eucapnic/hypocapnic CSA can develop into CSB if the loop gain (PaCO2
sensitivity) is too high, leading to an "overcompensation" in the ventilatory response [6, 30]. Figure
2.7 illustrates the "overcompensation" in the feedback loop present in CSB events.
==PAGE:24==
Chapter 2. Clinical Background
9
CSB is tightly associated with HF as studies have found that between a ﬁfth and half of all
patients with HF with reduced ejection fraction (HFrEF) has CSA, typically expressed as CSB
[22, 30, 31]. HF is a disease that can develop as a result of many other pathologies (e.g. atrial
ﬁbrillation, myocardial infarction and coronary heart disease). The severity of HF is measured
based on the left ventricular ejection fracture (LVEF), where HFrEF is deﬁned as LVEF < 40 %,
heart failure with preserved ejection fraction (HFpEF) is deﬁned as LVEF > 50 % and mid-range
heart failure falls between the two [30]. CSA in HF patients have been shown to be a predictor of
HF severity, to increase the risk of ventricular arrhythmia and to predict mortality but the inﬂuence
of CSB is not as clear as most studies have not made a separation of CSB and CSA. The studies
that have researched the role of CSB have found that nocturnal CSB is not as strong a predictor
of HF severity as CSA, and it still remains to be clariﬁed whether nocturnal CSB can be said to
have a detrimental eﬀect on HF in addition to the CSA eﬀect, as recent studies have even proposed
that CSB could be a compensatory mechanism to combat the detrimental eﬀects of SDB [21, 22].
It should be mentioned that CSB can be present in daytime for some HF patients, which severely
increases the mortality rate of the patient, but this type of CSB does not fall within the scope of
this report.
Figure 2.7: Illustration of the imbalance in the neg-
ative feedback loop causing CSB.
CSB have also been associated with sleeping at
high altitude due to reduced partial pressures of
gasses, and while this still reduces the sleep qual-
ity, it is a state that can be easily reversed, by
returning from the high altitude [5, 22].
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 3
Technical Background
This chapter goes through the technical theory behind the methods used in the current project
with an emphasis on deep learning and the implemented transformer model.
3.1
Signal Pre-Processing
Figure 3.1: Example of a piece-wise cubic interpola-
tion. Reprinted from [32]
Signal pre-processing is an essential part of
preparing a signal for data analysis.
Raw
biomedical signals are often inﬂuenced by noise
and artifacts making it diﬃcult to interpret the
data and most often it is only a speciﬁc part of
the data that is of interest to the application.
This section will present the theoretical back-
ground for the advanced pre-processing meth-
ods used in this project.
3.1.1
Interpolation
Interpolation techniques are used to upsample
a signal to a higher sampling frequency by esti-
mating a continuous function of the data based
on the existing data points and extracting new
data points from that function. Linear interpolation is the simplest type of interpolation where
the estimate of the function of the data is constructed by connecting the data points linearly. A
more complex interpolation could be the cubic spline interpolation. Cubic spline interpolation is a
process in which a third degree polynomial is ﬁtted piece-wise between samples of data to ﬁnd a
smooth line that passes through all data points (see ﬁgure 3.1) [33]. It can be described by equation
10

Pi(xi) = yi

has to be continuous in all data points and they supply 2(n −1) equations for a total of 4n −2
equations. The two missing equations can be deﬁned by a boundary condition such as a "not-a-knot"
boundary, where [34]:
P1(x) = P2(x),

(3.4)
3.1.2
Digital Signal Processing Filters
In digital signal processing (DSP), ﬁlters can be used to sort away unwanted information from
wanted information in discrete-time signals, such as noise or unwanted frequencies leaving behind
only the relevant frequencies. The two ﬁlters types in DSP are the ﬁnite impulse response (FIR)
ﬁlter and the inﬁnite impulse response (IIR) ﬁlter, which have diﬀerent properties, due to the way
they process the original signal. The output signal of a FIR ﬁlter depends only on previous inputs
as it can be described by equation 3.5a, and the output signal of an IIR ﬁlter depends on previous
==PAGE:27==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
12
inputs as well as previous outputs, as can be seen in equation 3.5b [35].
FIR :
y[n] =

(3.5a)
IIR :

(3.5b)
Where x[n] is the input sequence, y[n] is the output sequence, N is the ﬁlter order of the FIR
ﬁlter, K is the feed forward ﬁlter order of the IIR ﬁlter, L is the feedback ﬁlter order of the IIR, ai
are the impulse response and bi and ci are ﬁlter coeﬃcients.
The transfer function of each ﬁlter in the z-domain can then be written as:
FIR :

(3.6b)
From these formulas, it becomes apparent that the lack of feedback elements in the FIR ﬁlter
results in the denominator becoming 1. This property makes the FIR ﬁlter more stable than the
IIR ﬁlter since the coeﬃcients can always be set to less than 1 [35]. Another advantage of the
FIR ﬁlter is that the phase can be designed to be linear, thus avoiding unwanted phase delay. The
biggest disadvantage of a FIR ﬁlter compared with an IIR ﬁlter is that it requires considerably
more computational power and memory to implement to achieve the same sharpness at the cut-oﬀ
frequency.
Moving Average Filter and Savitsky-Golay Filter
Filters can also be used to smooth a signal to reduce the inﬂuence of broadband noise and/or reveal
the overall trends. One of the simplest smoothing ﬁlters is the symmetric moving average ﬁlter,
which can be viewed as a non-causal FIR ﬁlter that can be applied to time step i as:
y[i] = 1
M
(M−1)/2
X
j=−(M−1)/2
cjx[i + j]
(3.7)
where y[i] is the output, x[i + j] is the input, cj is the j´th entry in a weight vector of size M (in
the moving average ﬁlter all entries in c are 1) and M is the ﬁlter size. It is important to choose
the right ﬁlter size when designing a symmetric moving average ﬁlter as it will determine how much
the signal is smoothed and ultimately, choosing the wrong ﬁlter size can ruin the signal. The ﬁlter
==PAGE:28==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
13
can be applied to an entire signal by convolution, moving the designed ﬁlter window one sample at
a time [36].
Another type of FIR smoothing ﬁlter is the Savitsky-Golay ﬁlter which opposed to the moving
average ﬁlter doesn’t have equal valued ﬁlter coeﬃcients (cj in equation 3.7). Instead it has the
coeﬃcients c which are a result of ﬁtting a Rth degree polynomium to each window of size 2M + 1
of the signal using a least squares ﬁt, where R is most often between 2 and 4. The purpose of
using ﬁlter coeﬃcients c is that higher order moments are preserved better in the signal than with
a moving average ﬁlter. c can be found by using the polynomial ﬁtting model:
y(t) =

(3.8)
Where ar are polynomial coeﬃcients (not to confuse with ﬁlter coeﬃcients). The squared diﬀer-

X2 = 1

where tm = −M, −M + 1... −1, 0, 1...M −1, M and ym are the input data. The least squares

that can be shown not to depend on the input signal values but on the ﬁlter size and the degree of
the polynomium, R, instead. The ﬁlter is applied similar to the moving average ﬁlter by convolution
with the signal that should be smoothed [36, 37].
3.2
Machine Learning and Deep Learning
Machine learning (ML) is a ﬁeld that covers the design and use of algorithms which adjust and
optimize automatically based on the presented data. The ﬁeld can be split into supervised methods,
in which the algorithm adapts based on the discrepancy between a prediction and a label, and
unsupervised methods, in which the algorithm updates based on internal evaluation metrics without
labels. Supervised learning is the most used of the two groups and it will also be the focus of the
current report. Supervised ML is a broad term which covers the challenge of getting a computer
algorithm to improve a certain task by trial and error. It could be determining a number between
one and ten based on an image of that number. The algorithm will come up with a probability for
each number and based on the degree of the mistake, it will be adjusted to have a higher chance of
guessing correct next time, until a certain point where it saturates and stops learning.
The popularity of Deep Learning (DL) techniques has exploded exponentially in the last decade,
ﬁnding uses in everything from chat-bots to self-driving cars. The development has been pushed
forward by an increase in computational power as well as an increase in big data availability, and new
methods keeps on popping up. [12]. Of course, this scientiﬁc revolution has also found its way into
biomedical engineering, where DL is currently helping doctors detect cancer tumors, predicting
eﬀects of DNA mutations and reconstructing brain circuits [11]. While the implementations in
==PAGE:29==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
14
diﬀerent ﬁelds of study can be quite diﬀerent, the methods are in general the same. Deep learning
networks are a special type of supervised ML, which have proved eﬀective at ﬁnding complex data
patterns and solving advanced task which cannot be solved with traditional ML methods. It has the
advantage as many other ML algorithms that it is data driven and therefore involves a minimum
of bias. The backside is that it requires immense amounts of data in order to avoid overﬁtting
and that it is very computationally heavy to train. The rest of the chapter looks closer at the
fundamentals of modern DL methods, goes into the speciﬁcs surrounding Transformer networks
and introduces two supervised ML models called the support vector machine (SVM) and Logistic
Regression (LoR).
3.2.1
Fundamentals of Deep Learning
The ﬁrst neural network consisted of a simple one layer network called the Perceptron (see ﬁgure
3.2) with an input layer, and an output layer. This network was theoretically proven in the universal
approximation theorem to be able to solve all continuous functions by expanding the input size.
However, the results using this network were poor, in fact it was not even found capable of solving
simple tasks, which can seem confusing knowing the versatile success of neural networks today.
The network could be mathematically described as a ﬁxed number of linear operation followed by
a step function) [12]:
f(x) =
(
0
if 0 ≥PN
i=1 wixi
1
if 0 ≤PN
i=1 wixi
(3.10)
where x is the input vector, f(x) is the output, N is the length of the input vector and w is the
weight vector. This basic network structure of linear operations followed by an activation function
is still used as the basis of modern neural networks (NN), though the network architecture has
increased immensely in complexity. The reason for the problematic results were in large due to the
lack of a training method, which also kept the perceptron network shallow as opposed to modern
networks which have several layers [12]. All in all, while the perceptron bear similarities to modern
NN, some of the vital functions described underneath were not invented until much later.
Network Architecture and Activation Functions
Despite the proof of the universal approximation theorem mentioned above, neural networks have
evolved from the single layer perceptron into multilayer networks. This can seem counter-intuitive
since the perceptron was proven able to solve all continuous functions, but deeper networks (i.e.
networks with more than one hidden layer) have proven to be more eﬃcient at tackling complex
hierarchically structured problems as seen in the real world [12, 39]. The mathematical expressions
for computing the output, also called the forward pass, of a deep NN can be expressed as in equation
3.11 and illustrated in ﬁgure 3.3.

Modern NN can use a wide range of diﬀerent activation function to introduce non-linearity to
the system and it can be diﬃcult to choose the optimal function. Three of the most common are
the sigmoid function, the tangent hyperbolic function (tanh) and the rectiﬁed linear unit (ReLU)
visualized in ﬁgure 3.4. The sigmoid function squeezes the output into the range [0, 1], and is most

often used in the output layer of binary classiﬁcation network. The tanh ﬁts the output into the
range [-1, 1], and is used throughout the network. Lastly, the ReLU acts linear for all values above
zero and suppress all values under zero [12].

tions in deep learning.
Layers consisting only of the operations in

is an illustration of a network made up only
of this type of layer, also called a feedforward
neural network. Feedforward layers are one of
the cornerstones of DL and given their maxi-
mal degree of input connectivity they are ca-
pable of ﬁnding complex patterns in the data.
This high degree of freedom can however com-
plicate and prolong training and in worst case
lead to overﬁtting and/or an improper model ﬁt
[11]. Therefore, feedforward layers are used in
combination with other types of layers in most
state-of-the-art networks, such as a recurrent
neural network (RNN) layer or a convolutional
layer.
There are many diﬀerent types of RNN layers, but they all rely on the basic principle, that the
output at a certain time step depends on the input at all previous time steps as shown in ﬁgure
3.5. The computations can be described in matrix annotation as:

.

(3.12a)

(3.12b)
Figure 3.5: An unfolded basic recurrent neural network

Where xt are the input to time t, ht are the
hidden state to time t, yt are the output to time
t, σ is the activation function and Wx, Wh and
Wy are the weight matrices for the input, hid-
den state and output. The RNN layers are typ-
ically used with sequential data, like time-series
data or natural language processing (NLP), be-
cause of their recursive properties. The hidden
layer is, so to speak, able to "remember" earlier
events or previously processed words [11]. The
memory function can be achieved by many dif-
ferent RNN architectures and one of the most
popular ones is called the long short-term mem-
ory unit (LSTM). The LSTM was developed in
1997 and constituted a major improvement of
the original RNN networks as it overcame one
of the early RNN problems of vanishing gradi-
ents by processing the input according to the state of a forget gate, input gate and output gate
within the LSTM. The gated processing allowed the network to learn long term dependencies in
the data as it could learn to regulate what information should be kept in the gated states and what
information should not [12].
==PAGE:33==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
18
Convolutional layers are designed to process multiple data arrays from the same dimensional
space, like RGB images (i.e. [3,X,Y]), and when implemented, they can be perceived as a complex
ﬁlter bank.
The input into a convolutional layer is projected into feature maps in which local
patterns are found by using multiple kernels where each kernel has the same weights during the
entire convolution with the signal. The weight sharing is done because the statistics of adjacent
cells in array data are typically highly correlated and invariant to position, meaning that similar
local patterns can repeat themselves at several position in the data. Furthermore, weight sharing
considerably lowers the memory requirements of the processor and the network training time. The
kernels are typically of a much smaller size than the initial input data, reducing the amount of
connectivity per layer which keeps the computational load low and combats overﬁtting. The local
patterns are in turn enhanced, shared and connected, if successive convolutional layers are used,
especially if used together with pooling, which is the process of combining adjacent inputs to a
smaller output (see ﬁgure 3.6). The structure allows the layer/network type to ﬁnd more and more
complex patterns for each layer, since all patterns can be made up from a combination of simpler
local patterns. This hierarchical increase in complexity is advantageous as it reﬂects the reality of
most real-world signals and it has lead to an extensive use of CNN in image recognition, exempliﬁed
in ﬁgure 3.6 [11, 12]. Mathematically speaking, each convolutional layer consist of a kernel cross
correlation (or convolution if the kernel is ﬂipped), as shown in equation 3.13 for one dimensional
signals with input size [N, Cin, L] and output size [N, Cout, Lout] [40]:
output[Ni, Coutj] = b[Coutj] +
Cin−1
X
k=0
H[Coutj, k] ⋆I[Ni, k]
(3.13)
where N is the batch size, L is the length of the input sequence, I is the input signal/channels,
H are the kernels, C are the number of signals/channels and b is the bias.
Loss functions
In order to improve a neural network, the output of the network has to be translated into a
quantiﬁable error term using a cost function. The basic principle of a cost function is to compare
the output of the neural network with the desired output, and compute a number which reﬂects
the equality of the two. There are multiple diﬀerent cost functions to chose from which can be
divided into two groups, cost functions for regression problems and cost functions for classiﬁcation
problems. The two most commonly used cost functions are the mean squared error (MSE) and the
cross-entropy loss (CEL) [39].
The MSE is primarily used in regression problems where the desired network outputs are not
categorical but estimates of certain values. An example could be predicting the temperature over
the next 4 days. It be can be mathematically written as [39]:
C(w, b) = 1
N
N
X
n=1
|| tn −ˆy(wn, bn) ||2
2
(3.14)
==PAGE:34==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
19
Where w are the weights of the network, b are the biases, N is the number of training inputs, t are
the correct answers and ˆy is the outputs of the neural network. As can be seen from the equation,
MSE sums the squared diﬀerence of the network output and the correct values and divides it by a
factor of 2n. This works well in regression because the MSE equation is convex in the interval [-∞,
∞], allowing the function to learn by stochastic gradient descent (SDG) (see section further down
on optimization functions) [39].
The CEL is used in classiﬁcation problems, where the desired output is a class prediction. An
example of such a task could be classifying whether an image contained a cat or a dog, or in binary
language a 1 or a 0. The formula is constructed with inspiration from information theory, where
cross-entropy can be thought of as a measure of diﬀerence between two probability distributions.
This means that in order to use it in DL, the activation function of the ﬁnal layer has to be a
sigmoid or a softmax function, such that the output can be interpreted as drawn from a probability
distribution. With a softmax implementation the CEL can be described as underneath across a
mini-batch of the complete data set:
C(w, b) = −1

of K which are the number of classes that the network can predict, and the exception of t which
has to be one-hot-encoded. From that it can intuitively be seen:

Optimization Functions and Backpropagation
One of the most essential diﬀerences from the perceptron network to modern neural networks, is the
use of backpropagation as a training method to update the weights in the network. Backpropagation
is implemented together with one of many gradient descent methods which are methods used in
many ﬁelds to maximize or minimize the output of a function. These processes are done by ﬁnding
the gradient with respect to the function parameters and then adjusting the parameters in the
direction which minimizes the error term [41]. It is done in an iterative manner until a minima has
been found (see ﬁgure 3.7), and for that reason gradient descent methods are also called optimization
functions. Mathematically, the simplest gradient descent function, called batch gradient descent,
can be expressed as:
wt := wt−1 −η∇C(wt−1)
(3.17)
==PAGE:35==

20
Where w is the diﬀerentiable variable, ∇C(w) is the gradient of the cost function with respect to
w taken over the entire data set and η is the learning rate, which is a hyperparameter that controls
the step size by which to update the parameters (example of a small learning rate can be seen in
ﬁgure 3.7). The cost functions used in NN are stochastic functions since they depend on many
individual subfunctions evaluated at subsamples of the complete data set. The gradient descent
function can therefore be elaborated as:

As the gradient is propagated further back through the network, the gradient with respect to a
certain weight becomes a sum of the gradients through all possible passages from that weight to
the output. The complexity of these computations can be mathematically and technically reduced,
but that is outside the scope of this report.
==PAGE:37==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
22
So far only the batch gradient descent algorithm has been shown, but this is very seldom used
in deep learning today because of the time it takes to train and its tendency to get stuck in local
minima. To get around these two problems stochastic gradient descent (SGD) was developed:
vt = γvt−1 −η∇Ci(wt−1)
(3.28a)
wt := wt−1 −vt
(3.28b)
Where γ is an optional parameter called momentum, reﬂecting the amount of carry on between
each update. The primary diﬀerence between batch gradient descent and SGD are that weights
trained with SGD are updated after computing the gradients of a single subsample. This introduces
a stochastic behaviour that decreases the risk of getting stuck in a local minima. Furthermore,
because the weights are updated much more frequently, the network also trains much faster. On
the downside the SGD has a tendency to be very unstable, increasing the risk of exploding or
vanishing gradients, which is a phenomenon where the gradients keeps on increasing or decreasing
until the network crashes. A more optimal solution to both the SGD and the batch gradient descent
is to update the weights based on a group of samples in the batch, known as a mini-batch:
vt = γvt−1 −η
n
n
X
i=1
∇Ci(wt−1)
(3.29a)
wt := wt−1 −vt
(3.29b)
Where n is the number of samples in the mini-batch.

Kingma and Ba in (2017) [41]:

Where β1 and β2 are exponential decay rates. The main advantages of the Adam optimizer is
that it computes a separate learning rate for each weight parameter in the network, thus improving
convergence speed. It does so by taking an exponential moving average of the gradient (mt) and the
squared gradient (vt), adjusted by the decay rates (β1, β2 ∈[0, 1]). The gradient and the squared
gradient are initialized as 0, which introduce a bias towards 0 during the ﬁrst time steps that has
to be counteracted using equations 3.30e and 3.30f.
Regularization
Figure 3.8: a) Fully connected FFNN. b) FFNN with
dropout layers setting some weights equal to zero during
the forward pass and backpropagation. Reprinted from
Srivastava et al. (2014) [43]
Overﬁtting is a common problem when training
neural networks. It happens when the network
ﬁts the training data to well and doesn’t gen-
eralize, meaning it will have a high prediction
error if presented with unseen data. The easiest
way to avoid this problem is to introduce regu-
larization methods, like a dropout layer, which
limits the degrees of freedom for the network.
Dropout layers are very simple in design but
turn out to be very eﬀective, since they stop
speciﬁc neurons from only representing speciﬁc
outcomes.
Instead the probabilistic represen-
tations are spread out over the entire network.
Figure 3.8 shows how the dropout layer works.
Instead of full connectivity some of the neurons
are removed and the output has to be computed
from the remaining neurons before the networks weights are updated. The dropout mechanism is
only used during training of the network, and it is removed during testing and implementation of
the trained network [43].
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
24
3.2.2
The Transformer Network
The transformer network was invented in 2017 by Vaswani and colleagues [44] to improve the
ﬁeld of Natural Language Processing (NLP). As ﬁgure 3.9 shows, it is built as an encoder-decoder
network similar to many other networks in NLP, where the encoder constructs a representation of
the input and the decoder translates the encoder representation into the output goal of the task
(e.g. a text translation or summary). Instead of using a recurrent neural network as the primary
unit to process the sequential input, an approach was developed based on positional encoding and
attention, which will be explained in depth further down. When the paper was ﬁrst published,
it outperformed all other machine translation networks based on two popular hall mark data sets
[44]. Though the entire method is interesting only the parts relevant for the current study will be
reviewed in this project.
Figure 3.9: The original transformer network as de-
veloped by Vaswani and colleagues in 2017 with the en-
coder on the left and the decoder on the right. Notice
that the encoder and decoder layers (two biggest boxes)
can be stacked as many times as desired. Reprinted from
Vaswani et al. (2017) [44]
Positional Encoding
One of the key features of sequential data is
the relative position of samples within the se-
quence. In most networks processing sequential
data, this information is encoded in the RNN
unit, but in the transformer network it is en-
coded with sine and cosine functions of diﬀerent
frequencies. It is applied by constructing a po-
sition matrix of similar size as the embedded in-
put and then adding the two together. Adding
positional content to the embedding matrix al-
lows the network to learn the relative position
based on amplitude diﬀerence instead of recur-
rence. The position matrix can be computed
as:
PE(pos,2i) = sin

pos
100002i/edim

(3.31a)
PE(pos,2i+1) = cos

pos
100002i/edim

(3.31b)
Where edim is the total embedding dimension
and i is the index in the embedding dimension
and pos is the index in the sequence. This op-
eration gives the property that every positional
encoding (i.e. PEpos+n) can be represented as
a linear transformation of the ﬁrst positional
encoding (i.e. PEpos), which should make the
positional information easily accessible [44].
==PAGE:40==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
25
Multi-Head Self-attention
Figure 3.10:
Illustration
of Scaled dot-product atten-
tion showing self-attention
scaled with the square root
of the embedding dimension.
Reprinted from Vaswani et
al. (2017) [44]
Attention is a method that has grown immensely within NLP the last
couple of years. In simplicity attention is a focusing method developed
to ﬁnd the area of interest in a given input and assign it a higher weight
of importance [45]. It can be adjusted to ﬁt the desired application and
to be based either on the context or as in the case of the transformer´s
self-attention, on the input itself. Computing self-attention is begun by

3.2.3
Logistic Regression
LoR is one of the simplest types of supervised ML classiﬁers. The idea is to feed the input data
into a linear equation (see equation 3.35a) and then use a sigmoid function to squeeze the output
into the interval [0, 1] (see equation 3.35b).

function is both convex and diﬀerentiable and therefore, the gradient methods presented in section
3.2.1 can be used to ﬁnd the optimal weights and bias for the model.
When the model has been trained, new data points can be classiﬁed by computing P and checking
if the value is above or below 0.5.
3.2.4
Support Vector Machines
The SVM algorithm can be used for both regression and classiﬁcation but only the latter will be
reviewed here. The objective of the SVM classiﬁer is to ﬁnd the hyperplane that results in the
maximum margin of separation between two classes as can be seen in ﬁgure 3.12. Let n data pairs
of xn and yn constitute the data set where yn can take a the value 1 or -1. For the linear SVM any
hyperplane can be written as:
0 = w · x −b
(3.37)
Where w is the normal vector to the hyperplane, b is the bias and x is a set of points in space. If
the two groups are fully separable by a hyperplane, the hard margin method can be used to derive
the optimal hyperplane by setting up two constraints for i = 1, 2 . . . n:
==PAGE:42==

gradient update, α is adjusted to ﬁt the constraints (i.e. α > C is set to C and α < 0 is set to 0).
