==PAGE:44==
Chapter 3. Technical Background
29
It can be observed that with the dual form, the solution does not depend directly on w but on
the dot product of the input vectors xi and xj which can be understood as a similarity measure.
This has the advantage that the dot product can be exchanged with a kernel function (K(a, b)) to
make a transformation of the input vectors to a higher dimensional space such that a non-linear
problem can be solved. This operation can be done without actually transforming the input vectors
to the higher dimensional space since it is only the dot product that is of interest and this can often
be reduced to a simpler computation in the original dimensional space. Two of the most popular
kernels are the polynomial kernel and the radial basis function (RBF) of which the latter is written
out here:
K(a, b) = e−γ||a−b||2
(3.49)
where γ is a regularization term and a and b are data points.
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 4
State of the Art - Detection of
Cheyne-Stokes Breathing
This state-of-the-art report is a result of a literature search, that was done prior to the study on
detection of CSB in PSG data. A complimentary state-of-the-art review of HF classiﬁcation has
not been completed. The literature search strategy have been described in Appendix A.
The goal of the current study was to develop an automatic algorithm that could ﬁnd the exact
location of CSB events within sleep recordings. This diﬀerentiates the study from most previous
studies within this ﬁeld, which had focused on solely determining the presence of CSB, with the
purpose of using it in a diagnostic setting. Though this diﬀerence made it diﬃcult to draw inspira-
tion from existing methods, some studies had implemented interesting ways of extracting features
or classifying entities which was considered when designing the method used in the current study.
Furthermore, they had shown that diﬀerent modalities such as ECG, nasal pressure, temperature
changes, belt signals and oxygen saturation contain information on the presence of CSB. In this
chapter the methods of the most relevant studies will be described to give an overview of not only
the newest advancements, but also the general state of the ﬁeld. All articles will be presented along
with a ﬂowchart depicting the method used in the article to give a quick overview and allow for
comparison across studies. Sections 4.1 and 4.2 have been split up, based on the classiﬁers that have
been used, and section 4.3 contains articles that try to detect periodic breathing (PB) in awaken
subjects. The PB section have been included, because the amount of research done within the ﬁeld
is sparse and because the technical problem shares similarities to the one that the current study
tries to overcome, even though the patients were not tested while sleeping. Prior to the article
review sections there will, for clarity, be a short description of the diﬀerence/similarity between
CSB and PB.
Deﬁnition and link between CSB and PB
An event can be categorized as a CSB event according to the AASM [23], if both of the following
conditions are met:
30
==PAGE:46==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
31
• There are episodes of ≥3 consecutive central apneas and/or central hypopneas separated
by a crescendo and decrescendo change in breathing amplitude with a cycle length of ≥40
seconds.
• There are ≥5 central sleep apneas (CSA) and/or central sleep hypopneas per hour of sleep
associated with the crescendo/decrescendo breathing pattern recorded over ≥2 hours of
monitoring.
As opposed to CSB, PB is not deﬁned clearly in the literature or by the AASM. What is commonly
accepted is, that manifestations of PB results in a crescendo and decrescendo change in breathing
amplitude, but with no apparent requirement as to the amount of modulation amplitude.
4.1
Methods Using Neural Networks
Two articles in the literature have implemented neural networks as classiﬁers. Neural networks
have the advantage of being able to ﬁnd patterns in the data that in some cases cannot be seen by
humans and since manifestations of physiological phenomenons vary a lot, a neural network might
be the best solution, to ﬁnding the underlying patterns in the data [11, 30].
The utility of neural network in the diagnosis of Cheyne-Stokes respiration -
2003 [1]
A. A. El-Solh, U. J. Magalang, M. J. Mador, J. Dmochowski, S. Veeramachaneni, A. Saberi, A.
M. Draw, B. B. Lieber, B. J. B. Grant
Objectives: To develop an automated algorithm for diagnosing patients with CSB based on
pulse oximetry (SpO2).
Data description: The data included 213 sleep studies made on individuals that were referred
to be checked for sleep-disordered breathing (SDB). Of the 213 patients, 23 had CSB, 132 had
obstructive sleep apnea (OSA) and 58 had no evidence of SDB. All the sleep studies were annotated
by two sleep technicians. They did not divide the data set into a training set and a test set.
Method: The raw signal was pre-processed by a 3 second moving average ﬁlter and the outliers
were automatically removed by not allowing consecutive segments to have more than 4 % change
and to remove all samples that fell below 20 % oxygen saturation. The pre-processed SpO2 signal
was then split into segments of 20.84 minutes which were checked for presence of CSB. In each
segment, the power spectral density was computed using the maximum entropy method with the
model order selected by the Bayesian information criterion, and the frequency and magnitude of
the power were extracted for the two highest magnitudes. In addition, the frequency entropy was
computed as in equation 4.1, where F is all frequencies in the spectrum, m(f) is the magnitude at
a speciﬁc frequency and f is fractional representation of the total magnitudes. Finally, the oxygen
desaturation events below 2 %, 3 % and 4 % were extracted from the time domain as the last input
features to the classiﬁer. The classiﬁer consisted of a probabilistic neural network that had three
output; classes CSB-CA, OSA or no-SDB. It was trained and tested using 5-fold cross-validation.
==PAGE:47==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
32
Figure 4.1: Flowchart of the algorithm developed by El-Solh et al. [1]
Entropy = −
F
X
[m(f) ∗loge(m(f))]
(4.1)
Results: The results showed a sensitivity of 100 % and a speciﬁcity of 99 % when detecting
the CSB-CA patients. In total only 3 patients were misclassiﬁed of which one OSA patient were
predicted to have CSB-CA and two OSA patients were predicted to be in the no-SDB category.
Evaluation of article: The study classiﬁes almost all patients correctly but since it only reports
error values from the cross-validation, there are no certainty of the results. Testing the network on
a test set is necessary for proper interpretation of the method. The usage of multi-domain features
as input to a neural network is interesting, though features from the frequency domain limits the
temporal resolution, if they are used in the conventional way.
Pattern recognition of obstructive sleep apnoea and Cheyne–Stokes respiration
- 2008 [2]
G. Weinreich, J. Armitstead and H. Teschler
Objectives: To train and test a neural network as a diagnostic tool for apneas and Cheyne-Stokes
breathing using ﬂow-related spectral entropy.
Data description: A polysomnography (PSG) was performed on 37 patients with either con-
ﬁrmed or suspected SDB. All recordings were scored by visual inspection and 21 patients turned
out to have OSA or obstructive sleep hypopnea (OSH), 11 patients had CSB and 5 patients had
normal breathing in non-REM sleep (N-NREM). The data set was not split into a training set and
a test set.
Method: The raw nasal airﬂow signal was split into 3 minute segments and each segment was
transferred to the spectral domain by the fast fourrier transform (FFT). From the power spectrum,
the spectral entropy was computed using the same equation as in El-Solh et al. (equation 4.1 [1],
except Weinreich et al. [2] implemented the binary logarithm instead of the natural logarithm, as
deﬁned in Shannon’s entropy function. Several diﬀerent parts of the spectral entropy was analyzed
(e.g.
low frequency entropy, CSB entropy, etc.)
and a categorical vector was made based on
==PAGE:48==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
33
Figure 4.2: Flow chart of the algorithm used by Weinreich et al. [2]
threshold values. The categorical vectors were given as input to a shallow neural network with no
hidden layer and 4 output neurons signifying the 4 classes; OSA, OSH, CSB and N-NREM. The
algorithm was tested with 5-fold cross-validation.
Results: The total accuracy of the algorithm was 91.5 %. The lowest speciﬁcity and sensitivity
came when distinguishing between OSA and CSB, resulting in 90.9 % and 90.2 %. The best feature
for discrimination was the entropy between 0.04 Hz and the frequency with maximal power.
Evaluation of article: As with the study from El-Solh et al. [1], they only use a training set
and cross-validation, which makes the results untrustworthy. On the useful side, the study does
have an interesting approach of splitting spectral entropy up into several separate features, which
might reduce the amount of unnecessary information inputted to a classiﬁer.
4.2
Methods Using Heuristic Classiﬁers
Most of the articles that have tried to develop an algorithm for CSB detection have used a heuristic
classiﬁer. These classiﬁers have the advantage of being easily interpreted in a physiological context,
which is of value when the results have to be evaluated. The disadvantage is that they are not data
driven but constructed based on pre-made assumption of the physiological states, which reduces
the algorithm ﬂexibility and introduces bias.
eAMI: A Qualitative Quantiﬁcation of Periodic Breathing Based on Amplitude
of Oscillations - 2015 [15]
H. F. Tellez; N. Pattyn; O. Mairesse; L. Dolenc-Groselj; O Eiken; I. B. Mekjavic; P. F. Migeotte;
E. Macdonald-Nethercott; R Meeusen and X Neyt
Objectives: To develop an automated, novel method for detecting and quantifying PB events.
Data description: Two cohorts were used in this study, one measured at Antarctica and one
measured in a clinical setting:
1. The Antarctica cohort took place in Concordia (3.800 meters above the Ocean) and it consists
of 74 PSG recordings retrieved as repeated measures of 14 subjects (age = 39 +- 9.8) without
==PAGE:49==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
34
Figure 4.3: Flowchart for computing eAMI.
known comorbidities. The median Apnea/Hypopnea Index (AHI), Obstructive Apnea Index
(OAI) and Central Apnea Index (CAI) was 58.6, 4.8 and 26.5, respectively. The recordings
were annotated according to the 2012 AASM sleep scoring manual by a professional sleep
technician.
2. The clinical cohort were gathered in two diﬀerent sleep laboratories in Europe. It includes
39 PSG recordings from diﬀerent patients suﬀering from various sleep disorders.
A sleep
technician annotated all the recordings according to the 2007 AASM sleep scoring manual.
Method: The authors developed an approach based on amplitude modulation of either the
nasal airﬂow, thoracic movement or abdominal movement. The chosen raw signal was pre-processed
with a band-pass ﬁlter with cutoﬀat 0.125 Hz and 0.4 Hz, corresponding to normal adult breathing
(RSresp[n]). An amplitude modulation demodulation technique was used, taking the absolute values
followed by low-pass ﬁltering to get the signal envelope (RSam[n]). Lastly, the energy was computed
for both RSresp[n] and RSam[n] according to equation 4.2 and the results were passed into equation
4.3 to get the estimated Amplitude Modulation Index (eAMI) in which a value around 1 signiﬁed
an apnea event. A minimum requirement of an eAMI above θ for longer period than L = 2 ∗N
was required to detect PB. The signals were analysed in segments of size N. A clinical periodic
breathing index (cPBI) were deﬁned as the ratio between the total recording time and the sum of
the length of PB events. This was done to be able to compare AHI.
E[n] = 1
N
n/2
X
k=−N/2
| RS[k]2 |
(4.2)
eAMI[n] = 1 −0.5log(Eresp[n]/Eam[n])
(4.3)
Results: Diﬀerent values for θ and N were tested on the Antarctic cohort and the optimum was
found to be θ = 0.65 and N = 40 seconds, giving a maximum correlation coeﬃcient of 0.89 ± 0.018
(P < 0.01) between cPBI and AHI.
On the clinical cohort, the best results were achieved using the nasal airﬂow as the input with 93
PB events proposed by the algorithm, which were not detected by the sleep technician. Of the 93
events, 23 were PB events with complex variations of the respiratory signal, 50 events were PB
==PAGE:50==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
35
with less than 50 % variation in the signal and 14 events were false positives. On the Antarctic
cohort numerous amounts of non-scored events were found, but not further analysed.
Evaluation of article: The presented method shows a high temporal resolution when ﬁnding
the PB events, which is only achieved by one other study [16]. Unfortunately, the article does not
present any analysis of accuracy, speciﬁcity and sensitivity. The algorithm shows a tendency to be
oversensitive, but it should be further evaluated.
ResCSRF: Algorithm to Automatically Extract Cheyne–Stokes Respiration Fea-
tures From Respiratory Signals - 2018 [16]
F. Javed, H. Fox, and J. Armitstead
Objectives: To develop an automated CSB event detection algorithm using multiple modalities.
Data description: 49 patients with CSB and HF were tested.
Some of these patients had
conﬁrmed CSA whereas others did not have CSA. The patients were appointed to the hospital
where an overnight cardiorespiratory polygraphy was recorded, and later annotated, according to
the AASM 2012 scoring manual. The data set was split into a training set and a test set before
running the algorithm.
Method: Figure 4.4 shows how the diﬀerent modalities were pre-processed and utilized. The
low-pass ﬁlter had a cutoﬀfrequency at 1 Hz and the high-pass had a cutoﬀat 0.1 Hz. The signals
were passed forward and backwards through the ﬁlter to achieve zero-phase ﬁltering and thereby,
avoiding phase distortions. The linear interpolation was done to remove outliers in case the signal
dropped below a pre-deﬁned threshold.
The apnea detection module was based on a set of rules evaluating nasal pressure segments of 60
seconds. If the signal dropped below 10 % of baseline and fell within the minimum and maximum
length requirements, and event would be logged. The same module also detected hypopneas when
the signal dropped below 30 % of baseline. The abdomen and thoracic signals were used to extract
the type of apnea event by looking at respiratory eﬀort. Hyperpneas were found by computing
the signal envelope using the Hilbert transform. The upper envelope was then passed through an
auto-regressive ﬁlter to get the mean ventilation and a hyperpnea event was logged in locations
between adjacent apneas, where the upper envelope exceeded the mean ventilation. Lastly, the
CSB events were found following rules made according to the AASM scoring manual, and the CSB
features were extracted.
Results: The primary results from the study was presented as a comparison between the per-
centage of CSR per recording calculated by the algorithm and the sleep technicians. The mean
diﬀerences were -0.5 % and 0.5 % with reproducibility coeﬃcients (RPC) of 6.4 % and 8.1 % for
the training and test set, respectively. There was also a close correlation between the CSB features
extracted by the algorithm and the sleep technicians for the test set, with a mean diﬀerence of 2
seconds and a limit of agreement close to 6 seconds.
Evaluation of article: This is one of the two studies in the literature that determines the exact
==PAGE:51==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
36
Figure 4.4: Flowchart of the ResCSRF algorithm.
location of CSB events within a recording, and as such it takes on an extra layer of complexity com-
pared to the rest of the studies. The study achieves a remarkable correlation between the algorithm
and the sleep technicians in terms of percentage of CSR per recording but this is a weak marker
since CSR makes up only a fraction of the total recording time and it doesn’t specify whether or not
the events found were the same. A sensitivity and speciﬁcity analysis would strengthen their results.
Classiﬁcation of Cheyne Stokes Breathing and Obstructive Sleep Apnea Using
ECG - 2006 [3]
S. R. Suhas, S. Vijendra, J. R. Burk, E. A. Lucas, K. Behbehani
Objectives: To make and algorithm that could distinguish between OSA and CSR based on an
electrocardiogram (ECG) signal.
Data description: 20 individuals signed up for the study of which 13 had previously conﬁrmed
OSA and 7 had previously conﬁrmed CSB. The patients were tested with a PSG at a sleep lab and
the recordings were annotated by a sleep technician. Two separate groups of 15 minute recordings
were then created, one containing OSA in each recording and the other containing CSB. These
groups were further split into a training set and a test set.
Method: Only one lead out of the 12 ECG leads available, were selected for processing. The
raw ECG signal was pre-processed with a bi-directional high-pass ﬁlter with a cutoﬀfrequency
at 0.8 Hz, followed by a normalization and detrending process to get a zero-mean signal. After
pre-processing, the locations of the R-peaks were found. The R Wave Attenuation signal (RAW)
was then computed by extracting the amplitudes of the R-peaks, resampling at 10 Hz and using
the Welch method to get the Power Spectral Density. Trapezoidal integration was used to extract
the features RAR and RATN from the intervals 0.1 Hz - 0.5 Hz and 0.15 Hz - 0.2 Hz. A similar
approach was used on a heart rate variability series (HRV), which is a signal consisting of the
time interval between the R-peaks. The signal was resampled, normalised and the Power Spectral
Density was computed. HRTN was extracted by taking the integral between 0.01 and 0.4 Hz
==PAGE:52==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
37
Figure 4.5: Flowchart for Suhas et al. [3]
Results: The algorithm achieved sensitivity measures of 95.83 % and 87.5 % and speciﬁcity
measures of 79.16 % and 75 % for training and testing, using all three features.
None of the
extracted features worked well on their own, resulting in poor performance.
Evaluation of article: The study showed that ECG data might hold some information about
CSB, and potentially it can be used in addition to other modalities in future work. The same
author have also shown similar results in a classiﬁcation task between CSB and no SDB [14]. That
being said, the algorithm did not achieve a clear separation of the classes, which the authors argue
could be due to mixed segments of OSA and CSB, as well as some segments only containing a
fragment of SDB.
4.3
Studies of Periodic Breathing in the Awaken State
Both of the studies in this section have been included into this chapter because the method includes
a module that have not yet been tested as a part of CSB detection in the sleeping state.
Breathing Pattern Characterization in Chronic Heart Failure Patients Using the
Respiratory Flow Signal - 2010 [4]
A. Garde, L. Sörnmo, R. Jané, and B. F. Giraldo
Objectives: To develop an algorithm that could separate PB from non-periodic breathing (nPB)
in HF patients.
Data description: The breathing pattern of 61 subjects were measured in an awake state with
a pneumotachograph, of which 35 were healthy controls and 26 had HF. The recordings went on
for 15 minutes in a seated position, after which the patients were divided into groups according to
a clinical evaluation. 18 of the HF patients were put in a non-PB group and 8 patients were put
in the PB group, of which 3 patients had CSB while being awake and 5 patients had PB without
apneas. The data set was not divided into a training set and a test set.
Method: To begin with, the signal was pre-processed by removing outliers, ﬁlling out small
data gaps with auto-regressive (AR) modelling, low-pass ﬁltering and downsampling to 1 Hz. The
envelope of the pre-processed signal was extracted by employing the Hilbert transform, and the
==PAGE:53==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
38
Figure 4.6: Flowchart of the algorithm in Garde et al. [4]
frequency spectrum was calculated using AR modelling, to be able to analyse the modulation
frequencies. The peak modulation frequency (fp) was found and a discriminant band (DB) was
established in the frequency spectrum as fp ± 0.02 Hz. Three features were extracted from this
frequency spectrum; the power in the entire DB (P), the power in the right side of the DB (PR)
and the power in the left side of the DB (PL). The features were tested using leave-one-out cross-
validation on three classiﬁcation problems; HF vs. healthy, PB vs. nPB and nPB-HF vs. healthy.
Only the most discriminant feature were used for each classiﬁcation task.
Results: The main results from the study are summarized in ﬁgure 4.7.
Figure 4.7: The ﬁgure is taken from Garde et al. [4]
and shows the sensitivity (Sn), speciﬁcity (Sp), accuracy
(Acc) and the most discriminating parameter for each
task.
Figure 4.8: The ﬁgure is taken from Garde et al. [4]
and depicts the discriminant band for 4 diﬀerent subjects.
Evaluation of article: The study did not
achieve extraordinary results,
but the idea
of comparing the modulation frequencies does
carry some potential as can be observed in ﬁg-
ure 4.8, which indicates that the DB character-
istics aren’t similar for subjects from diﬀerent
the groups.
Evaluation of Periodic Breathing in
Respiratory Flow Signal of Elderly Pa-
tients using SVM and Linear Discrim-
inant Analysis - 2016 [5]
S. Argerich, S. Herrera, S. Benito and B. F.
Giraldo
Objectives: To determine if common clin-
ical variables and measurements of breathing
patterns can be used to conﬁrm the presence of
PB.
Data description: A data set was acquired
containing clinical data, ECG recordings and
respiratory ﬂow recordings with a duration of
==PAGE:54==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
39
Figure 4.9: Flowchart of the algorithm in Argerich et al [5].
15 minutes from 44 diﬀerent subjects. 25 of the recordings were categorized as PB and 19 as nPB
by medical doctors. The data set was divided into a training set and a test set.
Method: The respiratory signals were decomposed by the Complete Ensemble Empirical Mode
Decomposition (CCEMD) and the signal that most resembled the original signal was picked for
further analysis to have a more stationary signal for feature extraction. In the following step, the
power spectral density was found by using the Welch method and the features, absolute energy and
amplitude of the peak frequency, was extracted. Furthermore, a measure of the short- and long-
term correlation was extracted from the decomposed signal using a Poincare plot, and the Hurst
exponent was extracted with a 0.1 Hz window width to get a measure of the long-term changes
of the signal. A Support Vector Machine (SVM) and a Linear Discriminant Analysis (LDA) were
chosen as classiﬁers and they were trained with 5-fold cross-validation and tested on the test set.
Results: The best results were achieved with the SVM classiﬁer, reaching an accuracy of 88.2
%, a sensitivity of 90.0 % and a speciﬁcity of 85.7 %, when separating the patients into a PB and
a nPB group.
Evaluation of article: The use of a machine learning classiﬁer such as SVM or LDA has
potential to ﬁnd a data-driven optimum split criterion, which improves the eﬀectiveness compared
to some of the previously shown heuristicly based classiﬁers. Based on the shown results, it is
diﬃcult to interpret which features were most explanatory, since several of the clinical data features
are known to be predictive of HF as well as PB.
4.4
Summary of the literature review
All of the articles above solve the problem of ﬁnding CSB events in a diﬀerent way, as can be seen
by table 4.1. The studies from Javed et al. [16] and Tellez et al. [15] have the highest potential
from a clinical perspective with methods that detect CSB events with a high temporal resolution.
However, neither of the studies have computed sensitivity and speciﬁcity for their algorithm, which
weakens the results and makes it diﬃcult to compare them to others. El-Solh et al. [1] came up
with the best algorithm for classifying longer segments of data with a sensitivity of 100 % and a
speciﬁcity of 99 % when detecting CSB-CA.
==PAGE:55==
Chapter 4. State of the Art
40
The respiratory modalities, especially airﬂow, have been used the most in the previous litera-
ture, and they also show the most promising results. However, many other modalities have been
implemented to detect CSB and all with some degree of success. Whether these modalities can
be combined in a multi-modal input, is yet to be fully tested, and could be an approach for the
current study at hand. To this day, only Javed et al. [16] have used multiple modalities and shown
that the diﬀerent recording channels do indeed hold explicit information, that can be utilized in
meaningful ways.
The feature extraction in the reviewed methods are largely done in two ways, depending on
the scope of the algorithm.
The articles that have tried to detect the presence of CSB in an
entire recording, have experimented with features extracted from the power spectral density, such
as amplitude at peak amplitude frequency or spectral entropy. While these features can be very
useful, they have a low temporal resolution which makes them unﬁt to solve the problem of the
current study, unless they are combined with other features in an intelligent way. Developing a
smart way to use spectral features might be within the scope of the current research. If not, Javed
et al. [16] and Tellez et al. [15] have showed that brilliant results can be achieved using features
from the time domain.
Choosing the right classiﬁer is a crucial step towards getting a high performance. Most studies
within the ﬁeld have chosen a heuristic approach, setting up parameters and thresholds for classiﬁ-
cation. This approach limits the degrees of freedom, and given the recent advances within machine
learning, most algorithms would improve from implementing machine learning based classiﬁers such
as the SVM and LDA used by Argerich et al. [5] or a neural network. The two reviewed studies that
used neural networks were published more than 10 years ago, and the network methods they use
are not as eﬃcient as most modern networks. In addition, both networks were shallow, which were
probably a necessity given the relatively small data sets, but nevertheless, it limits the ﬂexibility of
the classiﬁer and its ability to ﬁnd patterns in the data. Still, El-Solh et al. [1] showed impressive
precision with their neural network classiﬁer, signifying that even better results might be achieved
with more data and a better network architecture.

==PAGE:57==
Chapter 5
Data Description of Multi-Cohort
Data Set
This chapter present the data set used for the current project. It is made up of 523 PSG recordings
from 4 diﬀerent cohorts; Sleep Heart Health Study (SHHS), MrOS Sleep Study (MrOS), Study of
Osteoporotic Fractures (SOF) and Multi-Ethnic Study of Atherosclerosis (MESA), which were all
accessed with the help and support of the National Sleep Research Resource (NSRR). The cohorts
were selected based on the availability of recordings in which a sleep technician had observed
periodic breathing (PB). Based on advice from the medical doctors and state-of-the-art literature
the following modalities were chosen to be used in the project:
• Thermistor measuring the temperature changes around the mouth reﬂecting airﬂow in and
out through nose and mouth.
• Nasal cannula measuring nasal pressure reﬂecting the airﬂow in and out of the nose.
• Thoracic belt measuring the chest expansion/contraction during breathing reﬂecting breath-
ing eﬀort.
• Abdominal belt measuring the abdominal expansion/contraction during breathing reﬂect-
ing breathing eﬀort
• Chin EMG measuring the muscle activity in the jaw.
• Oximetry measuring the peripheral oxygen saturation of the blood.
The nasal cannula signal was included even though it had not been measured in the SHHS
cohort.
This was argued to be the best solution opposed to reducing the available recordings
signiﬁcantly and worsen generalization by discarding the SHHS cohort or leaving out important
available information by not including the nasal cannula signal. Instead of a nasal cannula signal,
42
==PAGE:58==
Chapter 5. Description
43
the thermistor would be copied and act as a nasal cannula signal so that the network input size
was the same for all subjects. The thermistor, nasal cannula and both belt signals will for the rest
of the thesis collectively be called the respiratory signals.
Section 5.1 goes over the recording methods of each cohort and section 5.2 presents the data set
used in the current study.
5.1
Cohorts
The included cohorts were completed between 1995 and 2012 at diﬀerent locations, led by diﬀerent
researchers and for diﬀerent purposes. As a result, the recommended/used equipment and pro-
cedures alternate from cohort to cohort, and sometimes also within the same cohort. The units
presented for each modality are the same units presented by the NSRR and for some modalities
this means that the units are unknown. The modalities with unknown units as well as amplitude
manipulated signals will be seen as having an arbitrary unit (AU). Below, is given a brief overview
of the important similarities and diﬀerences between the parent cohorts.
5.1.1
Sleep Heart Health Study
The Sleep Heart Health Study was a study designed to investigate correlations between SDB and
cardiovascular diseases such as stroke, hypertension or coronary heart disease.
The study was
composed of a baseline measurement called SHHS visit 1 (SHHS 1) conducted between 1995 and
1998 and a follow-up measurement denoted as SHHS visit 2 (SHHS 2) conducted between 2001 and
2003. The inclusion criteria were: 1) minimum 40 years of age, 2) no previous treatment of sleep
apnea, 3) no tracheostomy and 4) no existing oxygen therapy. At baseline a total of 6441 subjects
were enrolled but only 3295 subjects returned for the follow-up experiment. Both visits consisted
of an unattended PSG recording, set up by a trained and certiﬁed sleep technician in the subjects
own home. Table 5.1 shows the recorded modalities from SHHS used in the current project, as well
as the corresponding sampling frequencies, hardware and hardware-ﬁlters [49].
In the current project SHHS 1 and 2 are considered as separate cohorts and all statistics are
computed separately for both visits. This means that two recording from SHHS1 and SHHS2 from
the same subject, are considered as two recordings from diﬀerent subjects to increase the data set
size. This split can be done because the signals are highly stochastic in nature meaning that two
recordings are bound to be dissimilar when recorded three years apart.
5.1.2
MrOS Sleep Study
Just as the SHHS, the MrOS database consists of recordings from two separate visits measured
from 2003 to 2005 (MrOS 1) and 2009 to 2012 (MrOS 2). The study was meant to shed light on
the correlations between sleep and several overall health parameters in the elderly male population
such as fractures, vascular disease and cognition. Recruited participants had to be; 1) 65 years
of age or older, 2) able to consent, 3) should be able to walk with the help of another person, 4)
could not have had bilateral hip replacement, 5) had to be able to self-report and 5) should not
be within risk of dying within the expected study time, according to the onsite investigator [50].
A total of 3135 subjects were recruited at baseline and from these subjects 2911 recordings are
available from MrOS 1 and 1026 recordings are available for MrOS 2. If possible, the recordings
were conducted in the subjects homes and the setup was prepared by a sleep technician. Table
5.2 illustrates the modalities from MrOS used in the current project, as well as the corresponding
sampling frequencies, hardware and hardware-ﬁlters [51].

5.1.3
Study of Osteoporotic fractures
The PSG recordings from the SOF study were retrieved between 2002 and 2004. Subjects were
recruited as a part of a longitudinal study dating back to 1986, which purpose was to explore the
rick factors associated with osteoporotic fractures in elderly women (above 65 years of age). In total
461 subjects went through an unattended overnight PSG recorded in the participants home. Table
5.3 illustrates the modalities from SOF used in the current project, as well as the corresponding
sampling frequencies, hardware and hardware-ﬁlters [52, 53].
5.1.4
Multhi-Ethnic Study of Atherosclerosis
The MESA sleep study is also a substudy of another longitudinal study.
The purpose of the
original study was to examine the development of subclinical cardiovascular diseases to clinical
cardiovascular diseases in an ethnically diverse population consisting of both men and women in the
age group 45-85 years. The sleep studies included unattended in-home PSG recordings performed

5.2
Data Set for CSB Prediction Algorithm
A total of 857 recordings with PB according to NSRR could be found in the four included cohorts.
Of these, 639 recordings were randomly chosen and checked for corruption. If > 50 % of the signal
was found to be corrupted based on the method described in section 6.1.4, the entire recording
was discarded.
442 PSG were left and these were selected to be annotated for CSB by three
external sleep technicians over two annotation rounds. In a scheme where 290 recordings were
annotated once by a single technician and 152 recordings were annotated by all three technicians.
The annotations were done according to the AASM manual for scoring CSB but with a slight
change proposed by medical supervisor Emmanuel Mignot in collaboration with several experts in
the ﬁeld. The alteration consisted of only requiring the cycle length to be 20 seconds long to make
sure that no events were missed (marked with bold fond) [23]:
A CSB event could be scored if both of the following conditions were met:

5.3
Inter-Scorer Agreement
The annotations were converted into true class vectors by constructing a zero vector of the same
length as the neural network output and then setting the vector values to one at the indexes where
the technicians had annotated CSB. For the recordings with multiple annotation ﬁles a consensus
annotation was found by sample-wise majority voting, which has been used to ﬁnd a consensus
score in other studies [13, 56]. Another way of ﬁnding the consensus score could have been to ask
the technicians to reach agreement on the annotations in unison, as done by Javed and colleagues
(2018) [16].
Earlier studies have revealed that there can be a big inter-scorer variability when sleep technicians
annotate respiratory events such as OSA, CSA, mixed apnea and hypopneas [13]. The same inter-
scorer variability was observed in this study and table 5.7 sums up the most important statistics
computed across the recordings annotated by all technicians. The soft f1-score corresponds to the
f1-score comparing the consensus annotations with the technicians annotation and the hard f1-score
is computed using the sample-wise consensus agreement of two technicians and comparing it with
the last technicians annotations.
5.4

MrOS visit 1 was used as the data set for the HF prediction algorithm as it contains 174 subjects
with self-reported HF or enlarged heart out of 2911 subjects 2. There was no information available
on severity of HF (i.e. LVEF) or enlarged heart and the patient group was expected to cover a

that you had congestive heart failure or enlarged heart?

==PAGE:64==
Chapter 6
Method - Cheyne-Stokes Breathing
and HF Classiﬁcation
Figure 6.1: Flow chart for CSB detection algo-
rithm.
This chapter presents the methods used in the cur-
rent project and the reasons for selecting them. The
ﬁrst four sections of the chapter revolves around the
main project, describing the pre-processing, the net-
work architecture, the post-processing and the train-
ing and testing paradigm in a chronological order as
they are used in the algorithm (see ﬁgure 6.1). How-
ever, the algorithm wasn’t developed as straightfor-
ward but instead in a more interactive way and it
should especially be emphasized that parts of the
pre-processing steps depended on the choice of neu-
ral network and the opportunities and limits that
came with it in terms of feature design.
The last
section in the chapter describes the methods imple-
mented in the extension project and it should be
viewed as a pilot project that can spark ideas and
work as a platform for future development.
6.1
Pre-processing
The objective of the pre-processing and feature ex-
traction was to make the data from the diﬀerent co-
horts comparable, to remove outliers, discard cor-
rupt recordings and prepare input features for the
neural network. The data ﬂow is visualized in ﬁgure
6.2.
49
==PAGE:65==
Chapter 6. Method
50
Figure 6.3: Flowchart of the resampling method showing the dependency on fsori.
6.1.1
Filtering and Resampling
Figure 6.2:
Flow chart for the pre-processing
step in the CSB detection algorithm. The ﬁrst four
blocks are visualized in ﬁgure 6.5
As was described in chapter 5, this project consists
of data from four diﬀerent cohorts of which two are
further divided into sub-cohorts. That makes a total
of six diﬀerent data recording settings with diﬀerent
sampling frequencies (fsori). In order to make the
data comparable across the diﬀerent studies, the ﬁrst
step of data processing was to resample it to a com-
mon adjusted sampling frequency (fsadj). A fsadj
of 8 Hz was chosen, making the Nyquist frequency 4
Hz which could seem unnecessarily high given that
normal breathing frequencies during sleep lie around
0.2-0.4 Hz [4]. The reason for this discrepancy was to
allow for a higher temporal resolution of chin EMG
activity, given EMG activity is much more transient
in nature than breathing activity [57, 58], without in-
creasing the computational load too much . Another
argument for setting fsadj so that the Nyquist fre-
quency was 4 Hz was to ensure that no information
in the signal was removed. While the state-of-the-
art studies presented in this report ﬁltered the signal
with an upper cutoﬀfrequency of 1 Hz or less for res-
piratory signals [3, 16, 59], other studies researching
SDB have used an upper cutoﬀfrequency around 4-5
Hz indicating that frequencies between 1 and 4 Hz
might contain information [60, 61]. Visual inspection
of the frequency spectrum of the data used in the
current study conﬁrmed that there were instances of
activity above 1 Hz, and early test showed improved
performance when including frequencies up to 4 Hz.
The process of resampling was done by applying
the minimally necessary combination of upsampling
techniques, low-pass ﬁltering and downsampling techniques, depending on the original sampling
frequency (see ﬁgure 6.3).
==PAGE:66==
Chapter 6. Method
51
Upsampling was done for modalities which had a fsori < 8 Hz as well as modalities where
8 Hz ∤fsori and all modalities were upsampled to the lowest possible value divisible by fsadj.
In the ﬁltering step of the resampling several factors had to be accounted for, depending on the
speciﬁc modality that was being processed: 1) As a result of the upsampling process, noise was
introduced into the signal and in order to remove as much as possible of this noise a ﬁlter needed
to be applied, so that only the frequency content of the original signal was present. This was done
by using a lowpass- or bandpass ﬁlter with a maximal upper cutoﬀfrequency at fsori/2 to account
for the Nyquist Theorem.
2) At the same time, the process of downsampling also introduced
requirements to ﬁltering in order to avoid aliasing. The ﬁlter prior to downsampling had to be
either a lowpass- or bandpass ﬁlter with a maximal upper cutoﬀfrequency of fsadj/2. 3) Lastly,
the signal had to be cleansed of power line noise at 60 Hz as well as baseline drift.
Following ﬁltering all signals in which fsori > 8 Hz were downsampled to 8Hz.
Respiratory Signals
If necessary, upsampling of the respiratory modalities were done by applying a piece-wise cubic
polynomial function and a FIR bandpass ﬁlter in order to satisfy all requirements. If the original
sampling frequencies allowed it, the upper cutoﬀfrequency was set to 4 Hz so that the resampled
signal contained all possible respiratory frequencies during sleep from bradypnea to tachypnea,
while removing as much unnecessary frequency content as possible. The lower cutoﬀwas set at
0.02 Hz to remove as much baseline drift as possible without suppressing the signal, and the ﬁlter
size (i.e. number of coeﬃcients) were set to 201 to ensure a sharp cutoﬀsince it was of high interest
not to distort hypoventilatory frequencies (i.e. between 0.1 and 0.2 Hz) and to avoid ripples in the
pass band. From the frequency response of the bandpass ﬁlter displayed in the top plot in ﬁgure
6.4, it can be seen that all relevant frequencies were left intact. The ﬁgure also illustrates the linear
phase response which was the reason, why an FIR ﬁlter was chosen over IIR ﬁlter for this project
though the same eﬀect could have been achieved with and IIR ﬁlter by passing the signal forth and
back through the ﬁlter (i.e. zero-phase ﬁltering). The group delay introduced by the FIR ﬁlter was
countered by padding the signal with (n −1)/2 zeros, where n is the signal length.
Oximetry Signal
The oximetry signal was upsampled using linear interpolation because piece-wise cubic interpolation
had a tendency to distort the original signal, especially around outliers. After that a lowpass FIR
ﬁlter was applied with a cutoﬀfrequency of 0.5 Hz and a ﬁlter size of 201 to remove added noise
from the interpolation step (see ﬁgure 6.4). As a last step, the signal was smoothed using a third
order Savitzky–Golay ﬁlter of size 101.
Chin EMG Signal
EMG activity measured with surface electrodes are present in a wide frequency band ranging from
10 Hz to 500 Hz [57, 58] but since all frequencies in this band are above the Nyquist frequency of
fsadj, they could not be represented by resampling to 8 Hz. Therefore, this signal was kept at the
fsori and highpass ﬁltered with a FIR ﬁlter of size 201 and cutoﬀ10 Hz, to prepare it for feature
extraction (see ﬁgure 6.4). No upper cutoﬀwas needed since the sampling frequencies of the chin
EMG in all cohorts were below 500 Hz.
==PAGE:67==
Chapter 6. Method
52
(a) Top: Respiratory modalities (airﬂow, nasal pressure and belt signals). Bottom: Chin EMG
(b) Top: Oximetry signal. Bottom: Movement artifact feature
Figure 6.4: Frequency and phase response of the FIR ﬁlters implemented after upsampling and prior to down-
sampling as well as the ﬁlter used to derive the movement artifact feature (section 6.1.5).
==PAGE:68==
Chapter 6. Method
53
6.1.2
Outlier Removal
Outliers were common in the data due to movement artifacts, sensor displacement, etc. and these
had to be removed since they did not carry any relevant information and they severely inﬂuenced
the subsequent normalization operation.
Respiratory Signals
Outliers in the respiratory signals, except for the nasal pressure, were removed by computing the z-
score for all samples and then smoothing the areas where the z-score > 6. The z-score is a measure
of how many standard deviations a given sample lies from the signal mean. A z-score of 6 was
chosen to only smooth the severe outliers as exempliﬁed in ﬁgure 6.5. A moving average ﬁlter with
a window size of 21 samples was applied from 30 samples before the outlier to 30 samples after the
outlier as the smoothing function.
The outliers in the nasal pressure signal had to be found with a z-score of 4 due to a higher
occurrence. In addition to the reduction in z-score, a heuristic rule was added to ensure that the
outliers were only smoothed if the signal amplitude was below 50 % of the outlier amplitude two
and a half seconds before to two and a half seconds after the outlier. This was done because the
outliers in the nasal pressure signal manifested as singular spikes compared to the often longer
segments of noise or consecutive spikes in the other respiratory signals.
Oximetry Signal
In the oximetry signal, samples were judged to be outliers if they fell under the lowest physiologically
possible value, typically due to a ﬂatline (i.e where a signal contains no activity) in the signal caused
by sensor dropout. This threshold was set to 60 in cooperation with medical doctors, reﬂecting a
SpO2 of 60 %. If a ﬂatline lasted for less than 35 seconds, each sample would be replaced by a mean
computed over the samples from 10 to 4 seconds prior to the speciﬁc sample. The threshold of 35
seconds was selected to avoid having to discard a lot of segments at a later stage because of the
presence of physiologically impossible values while limiting the portion of data that was adjusted.
Chin EMG Signal
No outliers were removed from the chin EMG signal.
==PAGE:69==
Chapter 6. Method
54
Figure 6.5: Example of the pre-processing step performed on a signal with outliers detected and smoothed.
Note that the axis amplitudes are diﬀerent for each subplot to visualize the outlier smoothing. The smoothed
outlier is part of a movement artifact.
6.1.3
Normalization
A normalization step was inserted to make the respiratory signals comparable across diﬀerent
subjects, diﬀerent cohorts and diﬀerent recording sites.
Respiratory Signals
The z-score normalization method was chosen over max value normalization and robust scaling for
==PAGE:70==
Chapter 6. Method
55
the respiratory signals and it can be mathematically described as:
f(xn) = xn −µ
σ
(6.1)
where xn is the n’th sample of the signal, f(xn) is the n’th normalized sample of the signal, µ is
the mean of the signal and σ is the standard deviation.
The method was chosen because of the output stability given the nature and origin of the signals.
The respiratory signals in the data set were very inﬂuenced by segments of reduced signal amplitudes
and ﬂatlines independent of SDB events, which made it diﬃcult to compare the absolute values
between subjects. This was further complicated by the diﬀerent use of equipment in each cohorts.
Therefore, it was decided that the normalization method had no need to retain information about
the absolute signal values, but it should keep focus on the intra-subject relative signal values.
Max value normalization was for long considered a good solution in the current project because
it squeezes the data into the interval [0, 1]. That property is beneﬁcial for neural network inputs
as it reduces the risk of very small or very big weights that lead to network instability, and it
in some cases increase the training speed signiﬁcantly [62]. The problem with using max value
normalization was that it could potentially mean normalizing based on the high amplitudes of an
artifact or a non-smoothed outlier, which could result in a too harsh reduction in signal amplitude.
Robust scaling overcomes this obstacles by normalizing based on the quantile range (25 % and 75
%), but it introduces the restriction that percentage of ﬂatline in the signal has to be lower than
25 % which was not the case for some recordings. In the end, z-score normalization was chosen as
it tackles both obstacles, normalizing based on the entire signal while allowing a high amount of
ﬂatline.
Oximetry Signal
The oximetry signal was normalized by dividing the signal with 100 to reduce the values to the
interval [0, 1], because, as mentioned above, these values are better suited as input to a deep neural
network [62].
Chin EMG Signal
The chin EMG signal was normalized with the z-score normalization, just as the respiratory signals,
but because the signal had a much lower standard deviation, the values were ampliﬁed much more.
To get values in the same range as the other normalized signals, it was chosen to divide by 10
following z-score normalization.
6.1.4
Segment Discarding Procedure
As described, some of the included recordings contained long periods of ﬂatline or heavily reduced
signal amplitude and it was desired to avoid training the neural network on any of these periods.
For that reason a script was written to automatically ﬁnd their positions to be able to discard
segments containing these periods at a later stage.
The operations done to the signals in this
section were carried out separate of the succeeding signal processing steps as illustrated in ﬁgure
6.1.
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 6. Method
56
Respiratory Signals
In the respiratory signals, these periods with very low signal-to-noise ratio were found by squeezing
the signals into the interval [0, 1] to make the amplitude comparable across diﬀerent recordings.
This was followed by ﬁnding all periods where the absolute value of the squeezed signal was > 0.03
for more than 2 min. This heavily reduced amplitude was chosen based on visual inspection of the
data, as a threshold under which the signal-to-noise ratio became too low. The 2 minutes length
requirement was based on advice from medical doctors stating that this was the maximum length
of an apnea.
Oximetry Signal
All periods still containing oximetry values below the physiologically possible, as described in the
paragraph on outlier removal, were deemed untrustworthy and discarded.
Chin EMG Signal
No periods were discarded based on the chin EMG signals.
6.1.5
Feature Extraction
16 feature vectors were extracted from the time domain of the pre-processed signals to be segmented
and fed into the neural network. An eﬀort was also made to derive features from the frequency
domain by doing the Holo-Hilbert Spectral Analysis of the respiratory signals and adding the
estimate of the instantaneous frequencies as feature vectors but the inclusion did not improve early
model performance and the features were removed [63].
The ﬁrst ﬁve vectors implemented were the normalized respiratory signals and the oximetry signal
to allow the network to get as much information as possible about the intra-subject amplitude
diﬀerences coupled with the signal shape.
The next four vectors were features describing the local variance in the respiratory signals. These
features were developed based on the fact that the variance during hyperpnea is much higher than
the variance during apnea, as can be seen from approximately the ﬁrst 160 seconds of the visualized
respiratory signals in ﬁgure 6.6. It was chosen over computing the envelope of the signal using the
Hilbert transform as other researchers had previously done [4, 15] because the Hilbert transform
method was more susceptible to sudden peaks in the signal. The vector was computed sample-wise
based on the standard deviation computed over a time period going from three seconds before the
sample to three seconds after the sample (see equation 6.2).
f(xi) =
v

(6.2)
Where f(xi) is the standard deviation feature value at time xi and µi =
1

k=−3fsadj xi+k.
After the outlier removal the respiratory signals still contained movement artifacts, represented
as periods of high amplitude noise or singular peaks of activity. To improve the probability that the
==PAGE:72==
Chapter 6. Method
57
Figure 6.6: Example of the variance feature computed over a segment annotated as CSB in the beginning
and normal breathing towards the end. The four subplots show the thermistor, nasal pressure, thorax belt and
abdominal belt signals.
algorithm could distinguish between these noisy periods of high apmlitude and hyperpneas, a feature
was developed that could unveil the position of the movement artifacts. The most straightforward
way would have been to implement a leg EMG signal where high amplitudes would be a sign of
movement but not all the cohorts involved in this study had recorded leg EMG. Instead, it was
argued that movement artifacts introduce broad band noise into the signal, why the artifacts would
be present in the high frequency bands where there were no presence of the breathing signals, as
can be seen on the spectrogram in ﬁgure 6.7. To take advantage of this the raw belt signals were
highpass ﬁltered using a FIR ﬁlter with a cutoﬀof 3.99 Hz and a ﬁlter size of 81, and then the
signals were compressed to the same size as the downsampled signals. This was done by taking the
average amplitude of the samples between every q sample where q = fsori/fsadj. The resulting
vector had a distorted frequency spectrum but it made up an approximation of the leg EMG signal
in the time domain, as can be seen when comparing the top and bottom plot in ﬁgure 6.7. The
method was used to extract two features, one based on the abdominal belt signal and another based
on the thoracic belt signal.
==PAGE:73==
Chapter 6. Method
58
Figure 6.7: Illustration of the link between movement artifacts present in the high frequencies of the abdominal
belt signals and muscular leg activity. All the plots show the same segment of a recording with presumed movement
artifacts. The top plot shows the raw leg EMG signals, the middle plot shows the highpass ﬁltered abdominal
belt feature and the bottom plot shows the spectrogram of the raw abdominal signal. Notice how the highpass
ﬁltered abdominal signal seems to increase in amplitude only when there is a movement artifact.
The feature vector using the chin EMG signal was computed similar to the vectors above repre-
senting movement artifacts, except for a max normalization step implemented post-segmentation
(see paragraph 6.1.6). The feature was developed in order to improve the networks ability to dis-
tinguish between OSA and CSA events because chin EMG is coupled with the oral breathing eﬀort
that is typical of OSA. Figure 6.8 illustrates this coupling by comparing the feature to two diﬀerent
periods of the same recording dominated by CSA and OSA respectively.
The last four feature vectors were computed post-segmentation (see paragraph 6.1.6) by doing
segment-wise z-score normalization of the respiratory channels. The output of this second normal-
ization step became a feature describing only the shape of the original vector with an amplitude that
was similar from segment to segment. The reasoning behind was that periods with reduced signal
amplitudes would result in very low network input values from the respiratory channels, leading to
a diminished inﬂuence from breathing parameters on the network output. Including these features
ensured that the shape of the respiratory signals had inﬂuence on the network output even though
==PAGE:74==
Chapter 6. Method
59
the amplitudes were very low.
The algorithm was tested both with and without these four features and the outcome is presented
in the results section. It performed best with the features included and therefore they were a part
of the ﬁnal model.
Figure 6.8: The chin EMG feature (plot 2 and 4 from the top) of two diﬀerent time periods in the same
recording, one with CSA events manifested as CSB and the other with OSA events. The Chin EMG activity is
generally higher during OSA events. Therm is short for thermistor signal and CSA and OSA are the annotated
events extracted from NSRR.
6.1.6
Segmentation
The signal was split into segments that could be passed into a NN. The choice of segment length
and overlap has to be understood in relation to the amount of samples classiﬁed per segment and
the deﬁnition of CSB. Before setting the parameters, it was decided that the ﬁrst and last minute
==PAGE:75==
Chapter 6. Method
60
Figure 6.9: Depiction of the developed embedding layer using two convolutional layers. TCF and Emb_size
are hyperparameters.
of the segment should not be classiﬁed because it would, in some cases, be impossible to determine
whether an apnea/hypopnea event in these parts of the segment were a single event or a part of
a CSB cycle (cycle length up to 60 seconds). At the same time, it was desired to compute three
classiﬁcation probabilities of each sample when testing the algorithm on an entire recording to
be able to sum the probabilities and increase the accuracy. This meant that the overlap had to
be equal to the fraction of samples classiﬁed per segment. Both requirements could be solved by
setting the segment size to 4096 samples, the overlap to 75 % and the fraction of classiﬁed samples
to the middle 3
4 of each segment.
6.2
Neural network
The deep learning network was the core of the algorithm developed for this project and many
diﬀerent design options were tested before settling on the ﬁnal network architecture. The following
section includes a description of the network architecture and the argumentation behind choosing it.
The hyperparameters of the network that were ﬁne-tuned to achieve the best possible performance
have been written in italic in the text and the parameter values of the ﬁnal model can be found in
chapter 7.
6.2.1
Network architecture
Several neural network architectures were considered for this project and they were evaluated
according to the following list of requirements:
• Embedding layer. The network was required to have an embedding layer to expand the
low dimensional data into a high dimensional representational space. Such a projection adds
ﬂexibility to the weights allowing the network to ﬁne-tune according to small input variations.
• Built-in memory function. The data for this project was time-series data, where each
consecutive sample depended on the previous samples. Therefore, no sample could be analysed
in itself but had to be seen in relation to the surrounding samples, and that required a network
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 6. Method
61

Complexity per Layer

Maximum Path Length

O(1)
O(1)

O(n)
O(n)
Convolutional
O(k · n · d2)
O(1)
O(logk(n))
Self-Attention (restricted)
O(r · n · d)
O(1)
O(n/r)
Table 6.1: "Maximum path lengths, per-layer complexity and minimum number of sequential operations for
diﬀerent layer types. n is the sequence length, d is the representation dimension, k is the kernel size of convolutions
and r the size of the neighborhood in restricted self-attention" – cited and reprinted from Vaswani et al. (2017)
[44].
with a built-in memory function. Furthermore, this memory property had to be able to look
far back into the signal to contain information of the ﬁrst CSB cycle when the third CSB
cycle was analyzed.
• Short maximum path length. The maximum path length is a measure of the maximum
number of operations needed to compare the two elements furthest apart in a single layer.
If the maximum path length is short it means that the network can compare distant sam-
ples/elements in a single layer without having to go through many unrelated computations.
If the maximum path length is long, the opposite is true [44]. For this given project, it was
important with a short maximum path length because the small data set size, relative to
most deep learning data sets, put a natural limit on the depth and complexity of the network
structure to avoid overﬁtting.
Figure 6.10:
Illustration
of the implemented trans-
former layer(s). Notice that
the layer can be stacked ac-
cording to the hyperparame-
ter layers.
Embedding of the network input is a well-known approach and in-
evitable method in NLP where every one-dimensional and categorical
input (e.g. a word) has a speciﬁc representation in a multi-dimensional
space. With inspiration from this procedure, a similar method was devel-
oped to make trainable representations of certain combinations of shapes
present in the continuous input feature vectors and project these repre-
sentations into a multidimensional space. It was done by implementing
two convolutional layers with kernel sizes of 2TCF in both layers and
a stride of TCF and TCF
2
in the ﬁrst and second layers respectively,
where TCF is short for a hyperparameter called time compression fac-
tor. The ﬁrst convolutional layer had input channels equal to the number
of network input features and 256 output channels and the second had
256 input channels and output channels equal to the hyperparameter
Emb_size (e.i. embedding size). The embedding layers are depicted in
ﬁgure 6.9.
RNNs have long been a popular choice when analysing time-series
data and they were considered for this project due to the built-in mem-
ory function, utilizing past inputs when processing current inputs. One
of the currently most popular RNN units is the LSTM unit. It has over-
come some of the early RNN problems of limited "memory", increasing
the amount of previous information that can be stored to help process the
current sample. The main problem with the LSTM is that its maximum
==PAGE:77==
Chapter 6. Method
62
path length is equal to the segment length, which as discussed previously has to be considerably
long for this particular project [11]. Transformer network units with self-attention layers overcome
this problem by allowing all inputs to a speciﬁc layer to relate to all other inputs, thus eﬀectively
making the maximum path length equal to 1 (see the self-attention in table 6.1). It does, however,
not have an inherent memory function like the RNN but that can be achieved by introducing po-
sitional encoding as described in the theory section [44].
Figure 6.11: Illustration of the net-
work architecture.
The convolutional
layers and transformer layers are shown
in detail in ﬁgure 6.9 and ﬁgure 6.10,
respectively. The axis are ﬂipped after
the transformer layer(s) to expand the
time dimension to its original size.
As mentioned, the Transformer network was developed for
NLP tasks where the sequence length is most often smaller
than the representational dimension, causing the complexity
per layer to be smaller for self-attention layers than for RNN
as shown in table 6.1. However, that was not the case for the
current project, where the sequence length was considerably
larger than the representation dimension, which pointed to-
wards using the RNN to keep the complexity down and com-
bat overﬁtting. Instead of discarding the self-attention, two
solutions were developed to overcome this obstacle. The ﬁrst
and primary solution was to utilize the stride feature in the
convolutional embedding layers to reduce the time dimension
prior to input into the self-attention layer. In ﬁgure 6.9 the
connection between the TCF parameter and the length of the
output of the developed embedding layers is shown and de-
pending on the size of TCF, this operation in itself can reduce
the sequence length enough to make the complexity per layer
lower than for an RNN layer.
The second solution was to
implement a boolean hyperparameter (Use_mask) that acti-
vated or deactivated a masking function in the self-attention
layer. If the mask was activated, then the complexity per layer
would decrease and the maximum path length would increase
according to the formula of the restricted self-attention layer
in table 6.1. The size of the neighborhood, r, (i.e. the part of
the signal not masked) was set to 1/4 of the input sequence
length. By including this boolean mask option it was automat-
ically tested in the training and validation paradigm whether
it improved or worsened performance.
As a conclusion weighing up the pros and cons, the layer
structure from the Transformer network was picked as the pri-
mary layer type of the network and a hyperparameter called
layers deﬁned the number of consecutive layers in the network.
The dimension of the Q, K, and V weight matrices in each
layer was set to be equal to the hyperparameter Emb_size
and it was chosen to use 8 heads in each self-attention layer
similar to the original study. The used transformer layer is
depicted in ﬁgure 6.10 and it should be noted that it is an adapted version that is ﬁtted to a
biomedical task instead of sequence to sequence NLP task.
==PAGE:78==
Chapter 6. Method
63
Figure 6.11 shows the data ﬂow through the network. After the embedding layers, positional
encoding and transformer layers, two feed-forward layers were implemented to allow full connectivity
and a softmax function was inserted to get a probability of each sample belonging to each class.
Before and after the ﬁrst feed-forward layer, the matrix was transposed to be able to expand the
post-convolution compressed time dimension to the original segment size.
6.2.2
Regularization
The transformer layer contains normalization steps after each self-attention layer and each feed
forward layer. The used normalization is called Layer Normalization and was developed by Ba and
colleagues (2016) [64] as a means of reducing time to convergence and ensure optimal amplitude in
the layer inputs. It is implemented in the network as:
f(zl
i) = α
 
zl
i −µll
σl + ϵ
!
+ β
(6.3)
Where f(zl
i) is the i´th normalized activated unit in the l´th layer, α is a trainable gain parameter,
β is a trainable bias parameter, zl
i is the i´th activated unit in the l´th layer, µl is the mean of the ac-
tivated units in the l’th layer and σl is the standard deviation of the activated units in the l’th layer.
Several dropout layers were implemented in the network to avoid overﬁtting and the dropout rate
hyperparameter was deﬁned as Dropout.
The batch size was set to 32 to achieve a balance between stochasticly updating weights to avoid
local minima and increasing the chance of representing all data tendencies in each batch.
6.2.3
Activation function, Optimization Function and Error Function
ReLU function was chosen as the activation function because the weights ﬂow easier and the risk of
vanishing or exploding gradient are smaller than in the sigmoid function and tanh function [12, 65].
Since the current problem is a classiﬁcation problem, the CEL was selected as the error function
and the error term was computed by passing the prediction probabilities through the CEL function
together with the true class vector. As mentioned earlier, only the middle 6
8 of the segment was
used to compute the error and the rest of the predictions were disregarded. The Adam optimizer
was picked as the step function due to the increased convergence speed compared with SGD and
the learning rate was the hyperparameter, LR [41].
6.2.4
Hyperparameter Search
To optimize the network a hyperparameter grid search strategy was used with the ﬁnal network
architecture. The grid strategy consisted of constructing a grid of possible hyperparameter values
based on previous successful hyperparameter settings from the intermediate network architectures
and the literature. All possible grid combinations were tested for 5 epochs with a stopping criterion
==PAGE:79==
Chapter 6. Method
64
(i.e. criteria that deﬁnes if training should be stopped early) of two consecutive increases in vali-
dation error measured every 400 training batches. Table 6.2 shows the hyperparameter search grid
with the selected hyperparameter combination in bold. The search results are further presented in
the results section.

6.3
Post-Processing
A post-processing procedure was developed to translate the segment-sized sample-wise output prob-
abilities into CSB predictions of complete recordings as illustrated in ﬁgure 6.17.. The procedure
required all pre-processed segments from a recording to be fed into the neural network and the
output to be ordered from ﬁrst to last segment. The overlapping parts were concatenated and the
probabilities for each class were added together so that each sample could be classiﬁed according
to the highest probability. The results was a prediction vector where isolated samples could be
classiﬁed as CSB surrounded by normal breathing and therefore, heuristics had to be implemented
to merge or delete CSB samples depending on the samples in their proximity. The ﬁrst heuristic
was to ﬁll out all gaps that lasted less than 2 seconds between samples classiﬁed as CSB. The second
heuristic was to remove all CSB events (i.e. consecutive samples of CSB prediction) that were less
than the minimum event length of 60 seconds. The third was to remove all remaining CSB events
that did not contain three CSB cycles. The number of cycles were computed by ﬁrst passing the
standard deviation feature vector from the two belt signals through a second order Savitzky–Golay
ﬁlter of size 101 followed by two iterations through a moving average ﬁlter with window size 101.
For each predicted CSB event the smoothed belt with the highest standard deviation was selected
to get the signal with the most distinguishable diﬀerence between peaks and troughs. Local peaks
and troughs were then found in the signal by ﬁnding the positions where the adjacent samples were
both smaller in amplitude or both larger in amplitude, respectively. It was ensured that there were
a minimum of 20 seconds (i.e. the smallest acceptable cycle length) between each peak and each
trough. If the segment did not contain at least three peaks and three troughs the segment was
==PAGE:83==
Chapter 6. Method
68
Figure 6.17: Data ﬂow and operations in the post-processing procedure. Explanations of every step can be
found in the text. Notice that the segment shown in step three is an example of a segment predicted as CSB but
where there weren’t three cycles why it was discarded.
discarded (see ﬁgure 6.21 for a visual representation). It could be argued that the number of cycles
should be based on the thermistor or the nasal pressure signals and this was initially the idea, but
visual inspection showed that these signals were too unreliable leading to unjustiﬁed discarding of
segments. The fourth rule was that if two remaining segments had a gap between them of less than
10 seconds they were merged leading to an output of a sample-wise prediction from start to end
with physiologically meaningful event morphology.
6.4
Performance Statistics
Performance statistics were computed as F1 scores and accuracy scores comparing the prediction
with the annotations over the whole recording except for the parts that had been discarded by the
algorithm. The f1-score was chosen as the primary evaluation metric as it takes into account the
==PAGE:84==
Chapter 6. Method
69
overweight of normal samples compared to CSB samples. It is computed as:
F1 = 2 Precision · Recall
Precision + Recall
(6.4)
Where Precision and Recall can be deﬁned as:
Precision =
TP
TP + FP
(6.5a)
Recall =
TP
TP + FN
(6.5b)
Where TP is true positives, FP is false positives and FN is false negatives. The f1-score was set
to 1 if neither the recording nor the prediction contained any CSB events.
To allow the reader to get a visual impression of the f1-score, two examples of the algorithm
output predictions and their respective f1-score are shown in ﬁgure 6.18.
Figure 6.18: Example of two algorithm output predictions. The top plot shows a prediction resulting in a
f1-score of 0.93 and the bottom plot a prediction with a f1-score of 0.63.
6.5
Training- and Testing Paradigm
The data set was split into a training, a validation and a test set consisting of 76.7 % (n = 339),
9.7 % (n = 43) and 13.6 % (n = 60), respectively. It was ensured that the validation and test set
==PAGE:85==
Chapter 6. Method
70
was composed only of recordings with consensus annotations, and except for this requirement the
recordings were distributed at random. After the split had been executed it was conﬁrmed that
all cohorts were present in all data sets as seen in ﬁgure 6.19. The distribution of recordings per
cohort in the test set should be noted for interpretation of the results. The test set contained 10
recordings from SHHS visit 1, 8 recordings from SHHS visit 2, 11 recordings from MrOS visit 1, 16
recordings from MrOS visit 2, 4 recordings from SOF and 11 recordings from MESA.
The neural network and the post-processing method was optimized while training on the training
and validation data in an iterative manner. The three models with the lowest validation errors
were selected and full recording analysis of all recordings in the validation set was performed for
each of the three models. The model with the best performance was chosen and tested on the test
set by analysing full recording results.
