6.6
Prediction of Heart Failure
This section describes the method used for classifying HF as well as the method used for a com-
plementary analysis of the correlation between HF and CSB using OR. Both methods utilize the
same feature extraction based on the predicted CSB event as illustrated in ﬁgure 6.20.
Figure 6.20: Flow chart showing the method progression trying to classify HF and to compute the OR based
on the CSB fraction feature.
6.6.1
Feature Extraction
As mentioned in the clinical theory section, earlier studies have shown that HF patients have a
higher prevalence of CSB and that severe OSA does not correlate with HF [66]. Based on that
knowledge, a set of features were designed to distinguish between CSB and severe OSA as well
as reﬂect CSB severity and these features were extracted from each segment found by the CSB
prediction algorithm.
The ﬁrst step before feature extraction was to ﬁnd the indices of each
hyperpnea period and the apnea/hypopnea periods in between. This was done similarly to the
cycle ﬁnding process in the post-processing (i.e. step 3) as illustrated in ﬁgure 6.21.
Figure 6.21: An example of ﬁnding the hyperpnea and apnea/hypopnea positions in a CSB segment. The read
dots represent the indices of the hyperpneas and the black dots represent the apneas/hypopneas.
Knowing the indices of the hyperpneas and apneas/hypopneas allowed the extraction of the
==PAGE:87==
Chapter 6. Method
72
designed features from each CSB segment:
• Number of cycles in segment. Computed as the number of peaks in a segment. It was
included as a reﬂection of the stability of the CSB event.
• Average cycle length. Computed as the average length from peak to peak. CSB events
most often have a cycle length of 45-75 seconds while repetitive OSA events can have a shorter
cycle length [22].
• Cycle length variance. Computed as the standard deviation of the distances between each
peak. The feature was included because of the hypothesis that CSB has a more regular cycle
rhythm compared to the erratic pattern of severe repetitive OSA.
• Energy in the belt signals around troughs. Computed as E = Pn
i x[i]2 where n is the
index from 5 seconds before the trough to 5 seconds after. The average of the energy around
each trough in a segment was computed as a measure of breathing eﬀort. The reason for
this feature was the fact that there is no breathing eﬀort during CSA, and as a result of that
neither in CSB, as opposed to OSA and mixed apneas [6, 20].
• Breaths before peak breath. The peak inspiration (i.e. local negative maxima) of the
nasal pressure signal was found in each hyperpnea period and the number of inspirations
prior to the peak with an amplitude above 50 % of the peak was counted.
This feature
was extracted because OSA is often followed by a deep inspiration called a recovery breath
as opposed to the waxing and waning pattern of CSB and therefore, it was expected that
there would be more breaths before peak in the hyperpnea period of a CSB event than the
hyperpnea period between repetitive severe OSA events [6].
• Breaths after peak breath. Computed similarly to breath before peak but with a count
of the number of inspirations after the peak. This feature was included to reﬂect the waxing
and waning pattern together with the breaths before peak feature. In the hyperpnea period
of a CSB event the amount of breaths before and after the peak breath would be expected to
be similar.
• Phase locking of belt signals. Phase locking was a feature developed by Al-Angari and
Sahakian in 2012 in study on detecting OSA [67].
The value is computed by taking the
imaginary part of the Hilbert transform of the two belt signals and using it to ﬁnd the
instantaneous phase:
θ(i) = arctan
 ex(i)
x(i)

(6.6)
Where θ(i) is the instantaneous phase, ex(i) is the imaginary part of the analytical signal and
x(i) is the original signal. The instantaneous phase diﬀerence between the two belt signals is
then found by subtraction and it is used to get the phase locking value (PLV) as follows:
PLV =

1
n
n
X
k=1
ei∆θ(k)

(6.7)
==PAGE:88==
Chapter 6. Method
73
Where N is the segment over which to compute the PLV. In the given project, PLV was
found by taking the average PLV of each apnea/hypopnea period in the interval from 5
seconds before a trough to 5 seconds after. The feature has proven to reﬂect the amount of
paradoxical breathing that can be seen in OSA [67].
• CSB fraction. Computed as the fraction of CSB samples in the entire recording. It was
included as it was expected that subjects with severe CSB would spend a bigger fraction of
the nights sleep experiencing CSB.
The features were averaged over all CSB segments in a recording by taking a weighted average
where the weights were determined by the amount of cycles in a segment divided by the sum of
cycles in all segments. The ﬁnal output was a feature vector of size 8.
6.6.2
Machine Learning Classiﬁer
LR was tested together with a SVM with a radial basis function kernel to test two linear models of
diﬀerent complexity. The LR was implemented with a L2 regularization term to avoid overﬁtting
so the loss function could be expanded to:
Logloss =
(x,y)
X
i=1
−yilog(P (i)) −(1 −yi)(1 −P (i)) + λa2
(6.8)
Where (x, y) are the number of input and output pairs, P (i) is the probability of the input be-
longing to the HF group, a are the weight vector of the LR described in equation 3.35a and λ is
the regularization parameter deciding the relationship between how well the model is ﬁtted to the
data and minimizing the weights. λ was set to 1 in the current project.
The SVM model was designed with the soft-margin method and the RBF kernel function. The
weight parameter C in equation 3.44 was set to 1 and γ in equation 3.49 was set to
1
features · input variance.
The input features were normalized using z-score normalization prior to classiﬁcation. This was
done because the amplitude range of the input features was quite broad which if not attended to
could lead to very high and very low feature weights. Both models were tested in a ten-fold cross-
validation scheme and they yielded similar average accuracy but with a slightly better performance
for the SVM. Therefore, the SVM classiﬁer was chosen as the preferred model but due to the lack
of separation in the cross-validation scheme it was decided not to generate and test on a test set.
6.6.3
Group Diﬀerence - Odds Ratio
Following the negative results of the classiﬁer above, it was decided to test whether there could be
found any correlation between HF and the most discriminative feature previously extracted. It was
decided to run this analysis similar to previous studies showing a link between atrial ﬁbrillation and
CSB as well as CSA [68, 69]. In the current study it was done by thresholding the CSB fraction
feature to split the subjects into two groups (i.e. high CSB fraction group and low CSB fraction
group) and then compute the odds ratio (OR) to tell if there was a diﬀerence in HF occurrence
from one group to the other. The formula for computing OR and corresponding conﬁdence interval
==PAGE:89==
Chapter 6. Method
74
(CI) was:
OR = ad
bc
(6.9a)
CIlower = eln(OR)−
p 1
a+ 1
b + 1
c + 1
d
(6.9b)
CIupper = eln(OR)+
p 1
a+ 1
b + 1
c + 1
d
(6.9c)
Where a is the number of HF patients in the high CSB fraction group, b is the number of no-HF
patients in the high CSB fraction group, c is the number of HF patients in the low CSB fraction
group and d is the number of no-HF patients in the high CSB fraction group.
The included data from MrOS visit 1 was split into two parts of equal size, a training set and a test
set. The training set was used to ﬁnd the optimal threshold value of the CSB fraction by which to
split into two groups. This was done by computing and plotting a receiver operating characteristic
(ROC) curve and selecting the threshold that resulted in the highest ratio of the True Positive
Rate to False Positive Rate. The subjects in the test set were then split into two groups according
to the chosen threshold and the OR and CI was computed.

Chapter 8
Discussion
This chapter contains the discussion of the results presented in the previous chapter and it has
similarly been split into a discussion of the main project surrounding the CSB prediction algorithm
and the extension project on HF classiﬁcation.
8.1
Prediction of Cheyne-stokes Breathing
This section starts of discussing the main ﬁndings in the study as well as the inﬂuence of recruiting
subject from multiple diﬀerent cohorts and two distinct patient sub-groups (severe OSA and PB).
Then it follows up with a discussion on validity and scientiﬁc interest of using the transformer
network structure on biomedical time-series data and a critique of the CSB annotations. Lastly, it
ends with a discussion of the future perspectives of the algorithm.
8.1.1
Main Findings
This study shows that it was possible to make an algorithm that could ﬁnd the exact positions of
CSB segments in multimodal overnight PSG recordings using an adapted version of a transformer
network. A F1-score of 0.76 was reached on the test set compared to an average f1-score of 0.83 for
the sleep technicians representing the golden standard and approximately the maximally achievable
f1-score. The algorithm was close to performing on the same level as the sleep technician with the
lowest f1-score of 0.78. As expected the performance was better on the validation set, where the
algorithm reached a f1-score of 0.84 compared to the average f1-score of the technicians at 0.85
and even outperformed one of the technicians. The drop in performance from validation to test
set was higher than anticipated and the reason might be that there were more recordings with a
low fraction of CSB in the test set and miss-classiﬁcations in these recordings were shown to be
punished harder than in recordings with a high fraction of CSB (see ﬁgure 7.2). The variation of the
f1-score between the sleep technicians was also much bigger in the test set than in the validations
set signifying that the test set constituted a more diﬃcult task. To overcome this problem the data
set should be expanded to have a more representative validation and test set.
Looking closer at the descriptive statistics of the predictions reveals that the algorithm has a
84
==PAGE:100==
Chapter 8. Discussion
85
tendency to predict too much CSB, on average predicting a CSB fraction of 0.31 per recording
compared to 0.27 in the consensus annotations with a R2 value of 0.96. The same tendency can be
observed by comparing the time of CSB. The high R2 values shows that the overshoot is constant
and it might in part be due to the post-processing process favoring long events compared to short
events due to the process of ﬁlling gaps between two CSB samples. The current algorithm ﬁlls gaps
separated by up to two seconds but it could be argued that the 2 second boundary should be reduced
because there is a higher probability that the samples predicted as CSB are wrong rather than the
15 samples in between. This argument is solidiﬁed by the fact that the f1-score was 0.02 points
higher prior to post-processing, an eﬀect that was not seen in the validation set. Instead of reducing
the 2 second boundary, another option could be to alter the process of ﬁlling gaps so that the class
of samples between two samples of CSB would be determined by thresholding the probability of
all the samples belonging to one or the other class. This would utilize the trained neural network
output and that might reduce the decrease in f1-score resulting from the post-processing.
The average length of the events and number of events was predicted with less accuracy. The
algorithm predicted longer but fewer events of CSB than the consensus score which might reﬂect
the fact that the algorithm was primarily trained on annotations of a single sleep technician and
tested on a consensus score between all three technicians. This argument can be backed up by the
statistics in table 5.7 showing that two of the technicians score considerably fewer but longer events
than the third technician, leading to lower errors during training when predicting long events.
The performance of the algorithm cannot be compared directly with state-of-the-art literature
because the data set used in the current study is unique. Furthermore, most of the studies in
the literature only try to classify segments of recordings into separate classes or patients into two
groups where the current study try to locate the exact position of CSB in the PSG. One of the
studies that did try to locate CSB was made by Javed and colleagues in 2018 [16] and they reported
a mean diﬀerence in CSB per recording of 0.5 % with a reproducibility coeﬃcient of 8.1 %. The
same statistics computed for the current algorithm was 2.0 % and 12.4 % which is a higher error.
However, this diﬀerence should be carefully interpreted because Javed and colleagues had a smaller
and much more homogeneous data set consisting only of HF patients with conﬁrmed CSB, a higher
average CAI and an almost half as low OAI, whereas the current study included subjects suspected
of CSB as well as subjects suspected of severe OSA. Their approach using a heuristic amplitude
based algorithm would be expected to perform worse on the diverse data used in the current study
because of the erratic pattern of severe OSA but that has not been conﬁrmed as it has not been
tested as a part of this study.
8.1.2
Severe Obstructive Sleep Apnea vs. PB
The algorithm performed worse when predicting subjects originally belonging to the severe OSA
group compared to the PB group.
This was expected as severe obstructive apnea manifest as
periodic patterns of apnea and breathing just as CSB but with the apneas being obstructive instead
of central [6]. The expectations were that the severe OSA recordings would contain much less CSB
than the PB recordings and that the algorithm would predict too much CSB, but the contrary
was the case. The included subjects with severe OSA had a higher fraction of annotated CSB per
recording than the PB group and more miss-classiﬁed CSB events which was the reason for the
decreased median f1-score and accuracy. This increase in CSB fraction might be attributed to the
much higher average AHI for the severe OSA group, but that does not explain the reason for the
==PAGE:101==
Chapter 8. Discussion
86
many miss-classiﬁcations. Instead, that might be explained by the elevated inter-scorer variability
in the recordings with severe OSA (see table 5.7) as more variability in the annotations decreases
the network convergence during training, ultimately leading to less accuracy in the predictions. On
top of that, the data set consisted of fewer recordings in the severe OSA group which could also be
a reason for a weaker network generalization to this problem.
8.1.3
Cohort Dependency
The f1-score turned out to deviate from cohort to cohort with SOF, SHHS visit 1 and MESA
resulting in the lowest f1-scores. The reduced f1-score in SHHS visit 1 and MESA could be explained
by the fact that the majority of the recordings from the severe OSA group belonged to either of
these two cohorts. For the SOF cohort, the explanation is less clear but the low f1-score might
be due to chance given the small and possibly non-representative data selection in the test set of
only four recordings. The SOF recordings contained the least CSB which, as preciously discussed,
increase the risk of low f1-scores.
The reduction in f1-score might also reﬂect a gender diﬀerence as SHHS visit 1, SOF and MESA
contain 3, 4 and 4 female recordings whereas MrOS visit 1 and 2 contains none and SHHS visit 2
contains only one. It has previously been suggested that there exists a diﬀerence in the the clinical
manifestations and severity of OSA between male and females due in part to the diﬀerence in
anatomy [70]. This might also be the case for CSB why it would be necessary to balance the data
set more in regards to gender to ensure an equal generalization to male and females - a topic that
have been highly discussed in other parts of DL research. This correlation have not been subject
to further investigation in the current study due to a lack of time and a too uneven distribution of
males and females which would result in too little power in the analysis.
The SHHS visit 1 and 2 was originally included to expand the data set and to strengthen the
analysis by showing that the algorithm functioned on this data base as well. It was done at the
cost of having to copy the thermistor signal to represent the missing nasal pressure signal, and
while both of these signals give an estimation of the ﬂow of air out of the lungs, they are not
alike. Therefore, it was expected that the algorithm would perform worse on recordings from these
cohorts but the algorithm only performs worse on the SHHS visit 1 and not on the SHHS visit 2,
and the reason for this detrimental performance might be explained by the sections above. Instead
of a performance reduction due to lack of nasal pressure signal, the network might have adapted
to overlook the nasal pressure signal because this was not consistent as it would sometimes be a
thermistor signal with a diﬀerent morphology. This hypothesis can be backed up by inspecting
the eﬀects of the ﬁrst convolutional layer in the network in ﬁgures 6.15 and 6.16. The top plot in
ﬁgure 6.16 shows the thoracic signal and the sum of all the activations after the ﬁrst convolutional
layer. By visual inspection it can be observed that the summed activations resemble the thoracic
signal (and the abdominal signal) and not the nasal pressure or thermistor, signifying that the
network is more dependent on these inputs. It is more diﬃcult to conclude the same based on the
example containing CSB in ﬁgures 6.12 and 6.13 because the nasal pressure signal resembles the
belt signals. The tendency of the network to focus on the belt signals might be a reason that the
algorithm doesn’t perform as well as the sleep technicians. It should be noted that an early version
of the model was tested without the SHHS data and it performed worse than the same version
of the model with the SHHS data which argues against the detrimental eﬀects of SHHS and the
missing nasal pressure signal being the cause of the lower performance. That could however, also
be a result of overﬁtting given the reduction in data set size when discarding SHHS. It would be of
==PAGE:102==
Chapter 8. Discussion
87
interest to increase the data set size and then exclude the SHHS data to test if the network would
improve performance and if the network would have an emphasized focus on the nasal pressure
signal.
8.1.4
Transformers and Biomedical Time-series Data
Transformer networks have revolutionized NLP the last couple of years but they have not yet been
implemented in many studies within biomedical engineering even though their potential is big. The
results presented in the current study adds to the proof that adapted transformer layers can be
used to analyze biomedical time-series data by achieving a satisfactory CSB prediction accuracy.
In excess to proving the functionality by getting the desired performance output, the transformer
layers also allows a depiction of the network layer focus by extracting the weight matrix of each
attention head. In other words, the relative importance of each data point when predicting a speciﬁc
data point can be illustrated. Figure 8.1 shows a heatmap where the x axis represent the inﬂuence
of all data points when predicting a speciﬁc data point (y axis). The complexity of the embedded
input matrix makes it diﬃcult to interpret exactly what the attention head focuses on but from the
ﬁgure it can be seen that there is a periodicity in the weighting, which might be a reﬂection of the
periodicity in CSB. If so, the transformer layer has learned to detect either periodicity of apneas or
periodicity of hyperpneas proving that the transformer architecture can be used to solve problems
in time-series data. However, more research is needed to ﬁnd a mathematical way of interpreting
the weights of multiple parallel heads and hold it up against the layer input to get the full picture.
Figure 8.1: Example of the self-attention weights in the transformer layer. Notice how there seems to be a
cyclic dependency possibly reﬂecting the periodicity in CSB.
==PAGE:103==
Chapter 8. Discussion
88
8.1.5
Annotations
Annotations done by certiﬁed sleep technicians are considered the golden standard of ﬁnding and
diagnosing CSB in PSG data, and in the current study annotations were used to derive the true class
labels for all data points. The annotations were visually inspected to get inspiration for features
and network architecture but in the process of visual inspection several scored CSB event were
discovered that seemingly weren’t CSB. This was conferred with medical doctors and conﬁrmed
by showing examples. Of course no conclusions can be drawn based on examples but a re-scoring
of the data would be necessary to determine whether the examples were some of many or just
a few wrong annotations in a vast amount data which is unavoidable. In addition to the wrong
annotations conﬁrmed by medical experts a couple of arguments points towards the presence of
faulty annotations in the data. For one, major diﬀerences can be found if comparing the annotation
statistics with state-of-the-art studies. In Javed et al. (2018) [16] they recruited only patients with
HF and conﬁrmed CSB and they found average fractions of CSB of 0.169 and 0.146 in their training
and test set, as opposed to the 0.31 and 0.27 in the validation and test set of the current study.
That is close to twice as much CSB in a diverse group of subjects where some were suspected of
having CSB and others suspected of not having CSB. In the group not suspected of having CSB,
the fraction was even higher with an average of 0.34. Part of this increase might be explained by an
almost twice as high average AHI though that diﬀerence in AHI comes from a much higher amount
of obstructive events instead of central events. Isolating the central events, the average CAI in
Javed et al. (2018) [16] was 6.1 and it was only 3.8 in the current study. Since a requirement for
CSB is that the apneas/hypopneas are central this is an indicator that there were a higher fraction
of CSB in the study from Javed and colleagues.
Another sign that the average fraction of CSB annotated in the severe OSA group (i.e.
0.34)
was too high was that 45 % of the subjects had a CAI of 0. This meant that CSB annotated in
these recordings could only rely on central hypopneas which can be very diﬃcult to distinguish
from obstructive hypopneas, ultimately reducing the chance of ﬁnding CSB. However, it should
be kept in mind that the original annotations regarding apneas and hypopneas from NSRR was
annotated up to 20 years ago and since then there has been a shift in the acknowledgement of
mixed apneas and the coupling between OSA and CSA. It is possible that it could inﬂuence the
amount of annotated OSA and CSA [6].
The fraction of annotated CSB in the severe OSA group could be a sign that the sleep technicians
had to much emphasis on the periodicity in the crescendo decrescendo pattern and too little focus
on the apnea type. This was also what the initial visual inspection and discussion with medical
doctors revealed but a ﬁnal conﬁrmation would have to depend on a re-scoring.
8.1.6
Future Perspectives
In addition to the changes already proposed in the discussion above, the algorithm could be im-
proved by enlarging the data set to allow certain method alterations. An increased amount of
data would reduce the need for removing outliers and discarding segments based on short lasted
artifacts and noise and instead make it possible for the network to learn to ignore these patterns
automatically. It would both reduce the amount of changes done to the signal in the pre-processing
and increase the average amount of segments that can be extracted per recording. More training
data would also make it possible to increase the currently small network complexity and depth
without experiencing overﬁtting which might improve performance.
==PAGE:104==
Chapter 8. Discussion
89
A re-scoring of the data would reveal whether the annotations could be trusted and it might
solidify the results. A faster, cheaper and simpler alternative to re-scoring the recordings could
be to hold the annotations up against the apnea and hypopnea annotations made available by
NSRR. By cross-referencing these two, the annotated CSB events that only contained OSA could
be removed.
Another interesting perspective for the algorithm could be to work on extracting the exact mean-
ing of the transformer layer weights as mentioned earlier. It would not only conﬁrm or discover
the decision rules with which the network classiﬁed CSB but it would open up the possibility of
using the network architecture exploratively to shed light on unknown correlations between sleeping
pattern and complex severe diseases such as HF.
If the discussed algorithm alterations would be done and the results conﬁrmed on a separate
data set, then the algorithm has the potential to be implemented as an assisting screening tool in
a clinical setting in the future.
8.2
Prediction of Heart Failure
It is important to emphasize that this analysis is done under the assumption that the CSB segments
predicted by the CSB prediction algorithm are instead segments containing PB characterized only
by the presence of periodicity in the signal. This assumption was made in cooperation with one of
the medical supervisors based in parts on the discussion presented in section 8.1.5.
It was not possible to classify HF based on the features taken from the CSB segments extracted
with the CSB prediction algorithm. The classiﬁer performed only slightly better than guessing
which is not surprising given that the average values of all features except two were very close. The
two features, CSB fraction and energy in belt signal around troughs, showed a diﬀerence in average
but with such a big variance that the two groups overlapped signiﬁcantly. One reason that the
features were so similar in the HF group and the no-HF group might be that features were aver-
aged across all CSB segments in a recording. Given that the hypothesis is that HF patients can be
classiﬁed on the basis of CSB presence it is crucial to the classiﬁcation that the extracted features
would reﬂect this presence. It has been shown in the literature and in ﬁgure 7.9 that HF patients
often exhibit both OSA and CSA during a nights sleep [6], why it could be argued that taking the
average of all segments would smooth out the diﬀerence between CSB and repetitive OSA making
it more diﬃcult to ﬁnd CSB. An alternative to averaging could be to take the features from the
segment that resembled CSB the most. It could for example be done by taking the segment with
the least energy in the signal around troughs.
Some of the features could also be improved, such as the feature meant to reveal crescendo-
decrescendo. A possible improvement of that feature could involve doing a cross-correlation of
the nasal pressure signal with a textbook hyperpnea segment from a CSB event which would re-
sults in a high correlation in CSB segments and a low correlation in segments with repetitive OSA.
Another possible reason that HF could not be classiﬁed based on the extracted features might be
that the diﬀerence in breathing pattern between HF patients and no-HF patients is not as clear as
anticipated. Figure 7.9 showed that while there are indeed more HF patients that have a high CAI,
the majority of the patients have a low CAI similar to the no-HF subjects. This could mean that the
==PAGE:105==
Chapter 8. Discussion
90
percentage of HF patients who develop CSB is too small to have an inﬂuence on the classiﬁcation.
The literature is not clear on this matter with articles reporting CSB prevalence between 50 % and
21 % among patients with HFrEF [22, 30, 31]. There was no information on the ejection fraction
of the HF patients in the current study and it is be possible that patients with HFpEF were less
susceptible to express CSB than patients with HFrEF which would make the diﬀerence between
the HF patients and no-HF patients less than what has been shown in the existing literature. A
diﬀerence that was even further diminished by the fact that the MrOS HF variable, and thus the
included HF patients, could also suﬀer from an enlarged heart instead of HF.
The underlying pathological reason for HF can be many diﬀerent diseases which might not have
the same inﬂuence on the breathing pattern during sleep and this factor has not been controlled in
the current study.
Lastly, it has been shown that severe repetitive OSA can trigger some of the same pathophysiolog-
ical mechanisms as CSB, such as increased loop gain, reduced CO2 reserve and chemical control
instability [6]. As a result of that no-HF subjects with severe OSA might have breathing patterns
that at times resemble CSB, which could explain the diﬃculty in the classiﬁcation task.
While the classiﬁcation was unsuccessful, the OR proved to be signiﬁcant when splitting the
subjects according to the predicted fraction of CSB. This result conﬁrms that there is indeed a
correlation between CSB and HF as others have previously shown [31, 66, 69] and indicates that
HF might be too complex a disease to classify based solely on breathing patterns during sleep.
==PAGE:106==
Chapter 9
Conclusion
In the current study an algorithm was developed in agreement with the project aims that could
automatically detect CSB in PSG data from four diﬀerent cohorts using advanced biomedical
signal processing and a transformer inspired DL network. The algorithm achieved a f1-score of
0.76 on the test set when comparing the predictions to a consensus score agreed on by majority
voting of annotations from three sleep technicians. This was only 0.02 points lower than the lowest
performing sleep technician and on the validation set the algorithm performed better than the
lowest performing sleep technician.
The algorithm was less accurate when predicting recordings from patients suﬀering from severe
OSA than recordings from patients suspected of having CSB and this was also reﬂected in the per
cohort f1-score.
The study works as a proof that transformer networks can be used on biomedical time-series data
to ﬁnd and recognize real-life patophysiological patterns. The network type might even increase
in accuracy if the size of the data set was increased as indicated by several factors including the
convolutional layers apparent focus on the belt signals.
The annotations of CSB that were made as a part of the current project showed signs of inaccuracy
and an excess of annotated CSB. This was backed up by discussions with medical doctors and should
be revisited in the future, possibly by re-scoring the recordings to reach certainty of its usefulness.
As an extension of the original project of detecting CSB, it was tried to classify HF patients
and no-HF patients but without success as the SVM classiﬁer only performed slightly better than
chance. To improve the results the feature designs should be revisited and the current averaging of
features should be changed. However, it is not certain that these alterations would serve to reduce
the classiﬁcation error enough to properly separate the two patient groups based on the available
data.

==PAGE:112==
Appendix A
State-of-the-Art Literature Search
Strategy
The ﬁrst step of the literature search strategy was to get an overview of the easiest accessible studies
on the subject. This was done by extensive searches on the scientiﬁc search engines dtu.ﬁndit.dk
and rex.kb.dk where the keywords displayed in table A.1 were mixed and interchanged to ﬁnd as
many diﬀerent relevant papers as possible. Original literature were preferred and review papers
were only used to get an understanding of the ﬁeld
SDB Keywords
Technical Keywords
Abbreviation Keywords
obstructive sleep apnea
detection
OSA
central sleep apnea
classiﬁcation
CSA
cheyne-Stokes breathing
deep learning
CSB
cheyne-Stokes respiration
machine learning
PB
periodic breathing
ﬁnding
SDB
sleep disordered breathing
locating
heart failure
segmenting
congestive heart failure
segmentation
analysis
automatic
heuristic
Table A.1: Keywords used in the ﬁrst step of the state-of-the-art literature search
After reading the articles and getting an overview of the ﬁeld, it was chosen to look into the
citations used in the retrieved studies and to track which papers had cited the most relevant
studies by using webofknowledge.com.
Furthermore, studies were shared in collaboration with
Villads Hulgaard Jørgensen to the extend that the studies could be used in both projects.
During the process a selection of studies were shared and discussed with co-supervisor Umaer
Rashid Hanif and medically relevant studies were supplemented by co-supervisors Prof. Emmanuel
Mignot and Prof. Poul Jennum.
97
==PAGE:113==
Appendix B
National Sleep Research Resource
Variable Deﬁnitions
The demographics and statistics on SDB described in chapter 5 were derived from the NSRR
archives. The deﬁnition of the extracted variables can be found underneath where the bold text is
the variable in question and the italic text is used to show which cohorts the deﬁnition belongs to
if variables with the same deﬁnition could not be extracted:
• PB was deﬁned as:
SHHS visit 1: Scorer observed periodic breathing for a period of at least 5 minutes during
study.
SHHS visit 2: Scorer observed periodic breathing for a period of at least 10 minutes during
study.
MrOS: Unusual occur - periodic breathing > 10 minutes.
SOF: Scorer observed periodic breathing for a period of at least 10 minutes during study.
Mesa: PSG QS: Periodic Breathing >=10 Min.
• OAHI was deﬁned as:
Obstructive Apnea-Hypopnea Index (OAHI) >= 3% - number of [obstructive apneas] and
[hypopneas with >= 3% oxygen desaturation or arousal] per hour of sleep
• CAHI was deﬁned as:
SHHS, MESA and SOF: Central Apnea-Hypopnea Index (CAHI) >= 3% - number of [central
apneas] and [hypopneas with >= 3% oxygen desaturation or arousal] per hour of sleep
• OAI was deﬁned as:
MrOS, SOF and MESA: Obstructive Apnea Index (OAI) all oxygen desaturations. SHHS:
Obstructive Apnea Index - number of [obstructive apneas] per hour of sleep.
• CAI was deﬁned as:
98
==PAGE:114==
Appendix B
99
MrOS, SOF and MESA: Central Apnea Index (CAI) all oxygen desaturations. SHHS: Central
Apnea Index - numer of [central apneas] per hour of sleep.
• AHI was deﬁned as:
Apnea-Hypopnea Index (AHI) >= 3% - number of [all apneas] and [hypopneas with >= 3%
oxygen desaturation] per hour of sleep

def
running_mean ( s e l f ,
signal ,
n) :
" " "
Smooth
s i g n a l
using
a running mean of
the
s i g n a l
with window
s i z e
n
Args :
−−−−−−−−−−
s i g n a l
:
np array
Signal
d e s i r e d
to
be smoothed by running mean
n
:
i n t
Size
of
window
s i z e
Returns
−−−−−−−
np array
smoothed
s i g n a l
" " "
# Pad
s i g n a l
to
ensure
the same
length
of
the
output
s i g n a l
( running mean
i s
a FIR←-
f i l t e r )
padded_signal = np . zeros ( len ( s i g n a l ) + n −1)
idx_start = (n −1)
// 2
padded_signal [ idx_start : −idx_start ] = s i g n a l
# Implement
running mean
cumsum = np . cumsum(np . i n s e r t ( padded_signal ,
0 ,
0) )
return
(cumsum [ n : ] −cumsum[: −n ] )
/
f l o a t (n)
def
get_sd ( s e l f ,
s i g n a l ) :
" " "
Compute the
standard
deviation
f e a t u r e . Any given
sample
in
the new
f e a t u r e
i s ←-
the
standard
deviation
taken
in
the
i n t e r v a l
from
sample
index −s e l f . sd_sample_margin
to
sample
index + s e l f . ←-
sd_sample_margin
Args :
−−−−−−−−−−
s i g n a l
:
pytorch
tensor
array
Signal
over
which
the
f e a t u r e
should
be computed
Returns
−−−−−−−
pytorch
tensor
array
standard
deviation
f e a t u r e
" " "
sd_sample_margin = 24
len_x = len ( s i g n a l )
sd_signal = torch . zeros ( len_x )
f o r
i
in
range ( len_x ) :
i f
i < sd_sample_margin :
sd_signal [ i ] = torch . std ( s i g n a l [ : sd_sample_margin + 8 ] )
e l i f
i > len_x −sd_sample_margin −2 :
sd_signal [ i ] = torch . std ( s i g n a l [ sd_sample_margin −8 : ] )
e l s e :
sd_signal [ i ] = torch . std ( s i g n a l [ i −sd_sample_margin : i + sd_sample_margin←-
] )
return
sd_signal

running_mean ( signal ,
n) :
" " "
Smooth
s i g n a l
using
a running mean of
the
s i g n a l
with window
s i z e
n
Args :
−−−−−−−−−−
s i g n a l
:
np array
Signal
d e s i r e d
to
be smoothed by running mean
n
:
i n t
Size
of
window
s i z e
Returns
−−−−−−−

Appendix C
170
np array
smoothed
s i g n a l
" " "
padded_signal = np . zeros ( len ( s i g n a l ) + n −1)
idx_start = (n −1)
// 2
padded_signal [ idx_start : −idx_start ] = s i g n a l
cumsum = np . cumsum(np . i n s e r t ( padded_signal ,
0 ,
0) )
return
(cumsum [ n : ] −cumsum[: −n ] )
/
f l o a t (n)
def
get_sd ( s i g n a l ) :
" " "
Compute the
standard
deviation
f e a t u r e
Args :
−−−−−−−−−−
s i g n a l
:
pytorch
tensor
array
Signal
over
which
the
f e a t u r e
should
be computed
Returns
−−−−−−−
pytorch
tensor
array
standard
deviation
f e a t u r e
" " "
sd_sample_margin = 24
len_x = len ( s i g n a l )
sd_signal = torch . zeros ( len_x )
f o r w in
range ( len_x ) :
i f w < sd_sample_margin :
sd_signal [w] = torch . std ( s i g n a l [ : sd_sample_margin + 8 ] )
e l i f
w > len_x −sd_sample_margin −2 :
sd_signal [w] = torch . std ( s i g n a l [ sd_sample_margin −8 : ] )
e l s e :
sd_signal [w] = torch . std ( s i g n a l [w −sd_sample_margin :w + sd_sample_margin ] )
return
sd_signal

# Compute the Odds Ratio and
Confidence
I n t e r v a l
f o r HF as a
r e s u l t
of :
# PB
Pos_class = (ML_input [ : ,
11] == 1) . nonzero () [ 0 ]
Neg_class = (ML_input [ : ,
11] == 0) . nonzero () [ 0 ]
# OSA above 20
Pos_class = (ML_input [ : ,
8] > 20) . nonzero () [ 0 ]
Neg_class = (ML_input [ : ,
8] < 20) . nonzero () [ 0 ]
# CSA above 5
Pos_class = (ML_input [ : ,
9] > 5) . nonzero ( ) [ 0 ]
Neg_class = (ML_input [ : ,
9] < 5) . nonzero () [ 0 ]
# CSB f r a c t i o n
above
0.3
Pos_class = ( csb_frac_test > thresh ) . nonzero ( ) [ 0 ]
Neg_class = ( csb_frac_test < thresh ) . nonzero () [ 0 ]
# Belt
energy
below
0.1
Pos_class = (ML_input [ : ,
3] < 0 . 1 ) . nonzero ( ) [ 0 ]
Neg_class = (ML_input [ : ,
3] > 0 . 1 ) . nonzero ( ) [ 0 ]
# Testing
i f PB i s
d i f f e r e n t
in
the two groups
a = len (( ML_input [ : ,
1 1 ] [ Pos_class ] == 1) . nonzero ( ) [ 0 ] )
c = len ( ( ML_input [ : ,
1 1 ] [ Neg_class ] == 1) . nonzero ( ) [ 0 ] )
b = len ( Pos_class ) −a
d = len ( Neg_class ) −c
OR = ( a∗d)
/
(b∗c )
# Testing
i f HF are
d i f f e r e n t
in
the two group
a = len (( hf_split_test [ Pos_class ] == 1) . nonzero ( ) [ 0 ] )
c = len ( ( hf_split_test [ Neg_class ] == 1) . nonzero ( ) [ 0 ] )
b = len ( Pos_class ) −a
d = len ( Neg_class ) −c
OR = ( a∗d)
/
(b∗c )
